-- Recipe 02: Ollama Proxy
-- General-purpose LLM query — proxy any prompt to any adapter/model.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 prompt="Explain quantum computing in one sentence"
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 prompt="Write a haiku about coding"
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m mistral prompt="What is 2+2?"
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter anthropic prompt="Summarise the history of AI"
--
-- Parameters:
--   prompt  (required)  The user prompt / question

PROMPT ollama_proxy
SELECT
    system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
    context.prompt AS prompt
GENERATE answer(prompt)
```

```spl
-- Recipe 04: Model Showdown
-- Same prompt to multiple Ollama models — compare responses side-by-side.
-- Uses parallel CTEs instead of a bash loop for a pure-SPL implementation.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/04_model_showdown/showdown.spl --adapter ollama \
--       prompt="What is the meaning of life?"
--   spl run cookbook/04_model_showdown/showdown.spl --adapter ollama \
--       prompt="Explain recursion in 3 sentences" \
--       model_1=gemma3 model_2=llama3.2 model_3=mistral

CREATE FUNCTION compare_responses(
    prompt TEXT,
    model_1 TEXT, answer_1 TEXT,
    model_2 TEXT, answer_2 TEXT,
    model_3 TEXT, answer_3 TEXT
) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
You are a neutral evaluator comparing responses from three AI models.

Prompt given to all models: "{prompt}"

First, reproduce each model's raw response exactly as given, formatted like this:

=== {model_1} ===
{answer_1}

=== {model_2} ===
{answer_2}

=== {model_3} ===
{answer_3}

Then provide your evaluation. For each model briefly assess:
- Response quality
- Key strengths or weaknesses

Conclude with which model gave the most helpful answer and why.
$$;

WORKFLOW model_showdown
    INPUT:
        @prompt  TEXT DEFAULT 'What is the meaning of life?',
        @model_1 TEXT DEFAULT 'gemma3',
        @model_2 TEXT DEFAULT 'phi4',
        @model_3 TEXT DEFAULT 'qwen2.5'
    OUTPUT: @comparison TEXT
DO
    -- Run the same prompt against all three models in parallel CTEs
    WITH
        response_1 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_1
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        response_2 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_2
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),
        response_3 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_3
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_3
        )
    SELECT
        response_1.answer AS answer_1,
        response_2.answer AS answer_2,
        response_3.answer AS answer_3
    INTO @answer_1, @answer_2, @answer_3

    -- Judge all three responses together
    GENERATE compare_responses(
        @prompt,
        @model_1, @answer_1,
        @model_2, @answer_2,
        @model_3, @answer_3
    ) INTO @comparison

    COMMIT @comparison WITH
        status   = 'complete',
        model_1  = @model_1,
        model_2  = @model_2,
        model_3  = @model_3

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT 'One or more models failed to respond.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```

     | Caller:  momagrid-hub
     | Adapter: momagrid
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 4

*   Key Strengths or Weaknesses: The poem's strengths lie in its concise and accessible language. However, the imagery could be more vivid, and the structure feels a bit abrupt.

Based on these evaluations, I would say that === phi4 === gave the most helpful answer. Its beautiful descriptions of nature and sense of wonder make it feel like a truly special piece of writing. While it may have some slightly repetitive or cliche moments, its overall impact is undeniable.

```spl
-- SPL 2.0: Self-Refine Pattern
-- Iteratively improves output through critique and refinement

WORKFLOW self_refine
  INPUT:
    @task TEXT,
    @max_iterations INTEGER DEFAULT 5,
    @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/05_self_refine/logs'
  OUTPUT: @result TEXT
DO
  @iteration := 0

  LOGGING f'Self-refine started | max_iterations={@max_iterations}' LEVEL INFO

  -- Initial draft
  GENERATE draft(@task) INTO @current
  LOGGING 'Initial draft ready' LEVEL INFO
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/draft_0.md', @current) INTO @_

  -- Iterative refinement loop
  WHILE @iteration < @max_iterations DO
    LOGGING f'Iteration {@iteration} | critiquing ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE critique(@current) INTO @feedback
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/feedback_{@iteration}.md', @feedback) INTO @_

    EVALUATE @feedback
      WHEN 'satisfactory' THEN
        LOGGING f'Satisfactory at iteration {@iteration}' LEVEL INFO
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @current) INTO @_
        COMMIT @current WITH status = 'complete', iterations = @iteration
      ELSE
        GENERATE refined(@current, @feedback) INTO @current
        @iteration := @iteration + 1
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/draft_{@iteration}.md', @current) INTO @_
        LOGGING f'Refined | iteration={@iteration}' LEVEL DEBUG
    END
  END

  -- If loop exhausted, commit best effort
  LOGGING f'Max iterations reached | iterations={@iteration}' LEVEL WARN
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @current) INTO @_
  COMMIT @current WITH status = 'max_iterations', iterations = @iteration

EXCEPTION
  WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @current) INTO @_
    COMMIT @current WITH status = 'partial'
  WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
    COMMIT @current WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

```spl
-- SPL 2.0: ReAct Agent — Population Growth
-- Fetch a country's population for the past two years via WebSearch,
-- then compute year-over-year growth rate using a Python tool (no LLM math).
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/06_react_agent/react_agent.spl \
--       --adapter claude_cli -m claude-sonnet-4-6 \
--       --allowed-tools WebSearch \
--       --tools cookbook/06_react_agent/tools.py \
--       country="France"

-- Tight prompt for search_population: return only the number, no narration.
CREATE FUNCTION search_population(country TEXT, year INT) RETURNS TEXT 
AS $$
Use WebSearch to find the total population of {country} for the year {year}.

Return ONLY the population as a plain integer with no commas, spaces, or text.
Example of valid output: 1409670000
No explanation. No units. No markdown. Just the number.
$$;

-- SPL PROCEDURE: search the web for a country's population in a specific year.
PROCEDURE search_population(country TEXT, year INT)
RETURNS TEXT
DO
  GENERATE search_population(country, year) INTO @result
  COMMIT @result
END

-- calc_growth_rate is a Python tool registered from tools.py via --tools.
-- CALL routes to Python directly — deterministic, zero LLM cost.

-- Tight prompt for growth_report: plain narrative, no tool use, no file writes.
CREATE FUNCTION growth_report(country TEXT, year_prev TEXT, pop_prev TEXT, year_curr TEXT, pop_curr TEXT, growth_rate TEXT) 
RETURNS TEXT 
AS $$
Write a concise 2-3 sentence population growth report for {country}.

Data:
- {year_prev} population: {pop_prev}
- {year_curr} population: {pop_curr}
- Year-over-year growth rate: {growth_rate}%

Plain prose only. No markdown headers. No file creation. No tool use. Just the narrative.
$$;

WORKFLOW population_growth
  INPUT:
    @country TEXT DEFAULT 'China',
    @year_curr INTEGER DEFAULT 2023
  OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
  @year_prev := @year_curr - 1

  LOGGING f'Population growth | country={@country} years={@year_prev}-{@year_curr}' LEVEL INFO

  -- Step 1: Fetch population for each year via WebSearch
  CALL search_population(@country, @year_prev) INTO @pop_prev
  CALL search_population(@country, @year_curr) INTO @pop_curr
  LOGGING f'Population fetched | {@year_prev}={@pop_prev} {@year_curr}={@pop_curr}' LEVEL DEBUG

  -- Step 2: Compute growth rate — routed to Python tool, not LLM
  CALL calc_growth_rate(@pop_prev, @pop_curr) INTO @growth_rate
  LOGGING f'Growth rate computed: {@growth_rate}%' LEVEL INFO

  -- Step 3: Compose the final human-readable report
  GENERATE growth_report(@country, @year_prev, @pop_prev, @year_curr, @pop_curr, @growth_rate) INTO @report

  COMMIT @report WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
  WHEN SearchFailed THEN
    GENERATE search_error_report(@country) INTO @report
    COMMIT @report WITH status = 'error'
END
```
     | 
     | Error: MomagridAdapter.__init__() got an unexpected keyword argument 'allowed_tools'
     result: FAILED  (0.2s)

```spl
-- SPL 2.0: Safe Generation with Exception Handling
-- Demonstrates LLM-specific error handling patterns

WORKFLOW safe_generation
  INPUT: @prompt text
  OUTPUT: @result text
  SECURITY: CLASSIFICATION: internal
DO
  GENERATE response(@prompt) INTO @result

  -- Quality check via semantic evaluation (LLM-as-judge)
  GENERATE quality_assess(@result) INTO @quality

  EVALUATE @quality
    WHEN 'high_quality' THEN
      COMMIT @result WITH status = 'high_quality'
    WHEN 'acceptable' THEN
      GENERATE improved(@result, @prompt) INTO @result
      COMMIT @result WITH status = 'refined'
    ELSE
      COMMIT @result WITH status = 'best_effort'
  END

EXCEPTION
  WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
    GENERATE response(@prompt) INTO @result
    COMMIT @result WITH status = 'conservative'

  WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
    RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1 LIMIT 3

  WHEN RefusalToAnswer THEN
    COMMIT @prompt WITH status = 'refused'

  WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
    COMMIT @result WITH status = 'truncated'
END
```

     | Caller:  momagrid-hub
     | Adapter: momagrid
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 3

```output
Encryption is the process of converting plaintext (readable data) into ciphertext (unreadable data) to protect it from unauthorized access. Here's a simplified explanation of how encryption works:

**The Encryption Process:**

1. **Key Generation:** A secret key is generated, which is used to encrypt and decrypt the data.
2. **Plaintext Conversion:** The plaintext data is converted into ciphertext using an algorithm (e.g., AES, RSA) that relies on mathematical operations (e.g., bit manipulation, modular arithmetic).
3. **Encryption:** The ciphertext is created by applying the encryption algorithm to the plaintext data using the secret key.

**Types of Encryption:**

1. **Symmetric Key Encryption:** Uses the same key for both encryption and decryption.
2. **Asymmetric Key Encryption:** Uses a pair of keys: one public key for encryption and a private key for decryption.

**Encryption Techniques:**

1. **Block Ciphers:** Divide data into fixed-size blocks, encrypt each block separately, and combine them to form ciphertext.
2. **Stream Ciphers:** Encrypt data in a continuous stream, using a keystream generated by a pseudorandom number generator (PRNG).

**Decryption:**

To decrypt the ciphertext back into plaintext, the same encryption algorithm is applied to the ciphertext using the secret key.

**Key Management:**

Proper key management is crucial for secure encryption:

1. **Key Storage:** Securely store and manage keys to prevent unauthorized access.
2. **Key Distribution:** Distribute keys securely to authorized parties.
3. **Key Rotation:** Rotate or update keys regularly to maintain security.

In summary, encryption converts plaintext into unreadable ciphertext using a secret key, protecting data from unauthorized access. The decryption process reverses this transformation to retrieve the original plaintext.
```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @prompt = Explain how encryption works
     |   @quality = This is a well-structured and informative quality assessment of the topic of encryption. Here are some specific strengths and suggestions for improvement:
     | 
     | Strengths:
     | 
     | 1. Clear and concise explanation: The text provides a clear and concise overview of how encryption works, including the encryption process, types of encryption, and decryption.
     | 2. Organization: The text is well-organized and easy to follow, with logical sections on key generation, plaintext conversion, encryption techniques, decryption, and key management.
     | 3. Technical accuracy: The text accurately describes various encryption techniques, such as block ciphers and stream ciphers.
     | 
     | Suggestions for improvement:
     | 
     | 1. Add more depth and examples: While the text provides a good overview of encryption, it would be beneficial to include more specific examples and scenarios to illustrate how encryption is used in practice.
     | 2. Include visual aids: Incorporating diagrams or illustrations can help to clarify complex concepts, such as how encryption algorithms work.
     | 3. Expand on key management: Key management is an essential aspect of secure encryption, but the text could benefit from more detail on best practices for key storage, distribution, and rotation.

-- Recipe 08: RAG Query
-- Retrieval-augmented generation over indexed documents.
--
-- Setup:
--   pip install "dd-vectordb[faiss]" "dd-embed[sentence-transformers]"
--   spl doc-rag add /home/gongai/projects/digital-duck/zinets/who-is-wen.md
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="Where did Wen grow up?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is Momagrid and why was it built?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is the Gang of Four in the ZiNets research team?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is eta_AI and how is it measured?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="How did Wen transition from physics to software engineering?"

PROMPT rag_answer
SELECT
    system_role('You are a knowledgeable assistant. Use the provided context to answer accurately.'),
    rag.query(context.question, top_k=3) AS background,
    context.question AS question
GENERATE answer(question)
```

```spl
-- Recipe 09: Chain of Thought
-- Multi-step reasoning: Research → Analyze → Summarize
-- Each GENERATE feeds into the next via workflow variables.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/09_chain_of_thought/chain.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="distributed AI inference"
--   spl run cookbook/09_chain_of_thought/chain.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 topic="quantum computing"

WORKFLOW chain_of_thought
    INPUT: @topic TEXT
    OUTPUT: @summary TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Research
    GENERATE research(@topic) INTO @research

    -- Step 2: Analyze
    GENERATE analyze(@research) INTO @analysis

    -- Step 3: Summarize
    GENERATE summarize(@analysis) INTO @summary

    COMMIT @summary WITH status = 'complete'
END
```

[10] Batch Test
     cmd : spl run ./10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter momagrid
     log : /home/papagame/projects/digital-duck/SPL20/cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test_20260327_090015.md
     | 
```bash
spl run ./10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter momagrid
```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 10: Batch Test
-- Automated testing of key cookbook recipes across multiple models.
-- Uses parallel CTEs to fan out all recipe × model combinations at once,
-- then generates a structured PASS/FAIL report.
--
-- The bash script (batch_test.sh) is kept for CI/CD and shell-based pipelines.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama
--   spl run cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama \
--       model_1=gemma3 model_2=mistral

CREATE FUNCTION summarize_results(
    model_1 TEXT,
    model_2 TEXT,
    r1_name TEXT, r1_m1 TEXT, r1_m2 TEXT,
    r2_name TEXT, r2_m1 TEXT, r2_m2 TEXT,
    r3_name TEXT, r3_m1 TEXT, r3_m2 TEXT
) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
You are a test reporter. Review the outputs below and produce a concise pass/fail report.

A result is PASS if the output is a non-empty, coherent response.
A result is FAIL if the output is empty, contains an error, or is clearly nonsensical.

Models under test: {model_1}, {model_2}

Recipe: {r1_name}
  {model_1}: {r1_m1}
  {model_2}: {r1_m2}

Recipe: {r2_name}
  {model_1}: {r2_m1}
  {model_2}: {r2_m2}

Recipe: {r3_name}
  {model_1}: {r3_m1}
  {model_2}: {r3_m2}

Format each line as:
  PASS  recipe_name  ({model})
  FAIL  recipe_name  ({model})

End with a summary line: "Results: X/Y passed, Z failed"
$$;

WORKFLOW batch_test
    INPUT:
        @model_1 TEXT DEFAULT 'gemma3',
        @model_2 TEXT DEFAULT 'llama3.2'
    OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
    -- Fan out all recipe × model combinations in parallel CTEs
    WITH
        -- Recipe 01: Hello World
        hello_m1 AS (
            PROMPT hello_world
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful assistant. Say hello briefly.')
            GENERATE greeting()
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        hello_m2 AS (
            PROMPT hello_world
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful assistant. Say hello briefly.')
            GENERATE greeting()
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),

        -- Recipe 02: Ollama Proxy
        proxy_m1 AS (
            PROMPT ollama_proxy
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                'hello' AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        proxy_m2 AS (
            PROMPT ollama_proxy
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                'hello' AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),

        -- Recipe 03: Multilingual
        multi_m1 AS (
            PROMPT multilingual
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a multilingual assistant.'),
                'hello' AS user_input,
                'English' AS lang
            GENERATE response(user_input, lang)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        multi_m2 AS (
            PROMPT multilingual
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a multilingual assistant.'),
                'hello' AS user_input,
                'English' AS lang
            GENERATE response(user_input, lang)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        )

    SELECT
        hello_m1.greeting  AS hello_m1,  hello_m2.greeting  AS hello_m2,
        proxy_m1.answer    AS proxy_m1,  proxy_m2.answer    AS proxy_m2,
        multi_m1.response  AS multi_m1,  multi_m2.response  AS multi_m2
    INTO @hello_m1, @hello_m2,
         @proxy_m1, @proxy_m2,
         @multi_m1, @multi_m2

    -- Generate the structured pass/fail report
    GENERATE summarize_results(
        @model_1, @model_2,
        '01_hello_world/hello.spl',        @hello_m1, @hello_m2,
        '02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl',        @proxy_m1, @proxy_m2,
        '03_multilingual/multilingual.spl', @multi_m1, @multi_m2
    ) INTO @report

    COMMIT @report WITH
        status  = 'complete',
        model_1 = @model_1,
        model_2 = @model_2

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT 'Batch test failed during generation.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Caller:  momagrid-hub
     | Adapter: momagrid
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 7
     | Tokens: 559 in / 346 out
     | Latency: 20507ms
     | Timestamp: 2026-03-27 09:00:35
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output
Here is the pass/fail report:

 PASS  01_hello_world/hello.spl  (gemma3)
 FAIL  01_hello_world/hello.spl  (llama3.2)

 PASS  02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl  (gemma3)
 FAIL  02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl  (llama3.2)

 PASS  03_multilingual/multilingual.spl  (gemma3)
 FAIL  03_multilingual/multilingual.spl  (llama3.2)

Results: 1/4 passed, 3 failed
```

```spl
-- Recipe 11: Debate Arena
-- Two LLM personas argue opposing sides of a topic, then a judge picks the winner.
-- Demonstrates multi-perspective reasoning, adversarial generation, and semantic evaluation.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="AI should be open-sourced"
--   spl run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 topic="Remote work is better than office work"
--   spl run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama topic="Tabs are better than spaces"

CREATE FUNCTION pro_argument(topic TEXT, previous TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
You are a skilled debate champion arguing STRONGLY IN FAVOR of the following motion:

Motion: "{topic}"

Your opponent's last argument (or "opening statement" if this is your first turn):
{previous}

Write a focused, persuasive argument supporting the motion. If this is a rebuttal round,
directly address and counter your opponent's points. Be concise (3-5 paragraphs).
Do NOT offer balanced views — you are arguing one side.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION con_argument(topic TEXT, previous TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
You are a skilled debate champion arguing STRONGLY AGAINST the following motion:

Motion: "{topic}"

Your opponent's last argument (or "opening statement" if this is your first turn):
{previous}

Write a focused, persuasive argument opposing the motion. If this is a rebuttal round,
directly address and counter your opponent's points. Be concise (3-5 paragraphs).
Do NOT offer balanced views — you are arguing one side.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION judge_debate(topic TEXT, pro_history TEXT, con_history TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
You are an impartial debate judge evaluating the following debate.

Motion: "{topic}"

--- PRO SIDE (arguing IN FAVOR) ---
{pro_history}

--- CON SIDE (arguing AGAINST) ---
{con_history}

Evaluate the debate on these criteria:
1. Strength of arguments
2. Quality of rebuttals
3. Clarity and persuasiveness

Declare a winner (PRO or CON) and explain your reasoning in 2-3 paragraphs.
$$;

WORKFLOW debate_arena
    INPUT:
        @topic TEXT,
        @max_rounds INTEGER DEFAULT 3,
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/11_debate_arena/logs'
    OUTPUT: @verdict TEXT
DO
    @round := 0
    @pro_history := ''
    @con_history := ''

    LOGGING f'Debate started | topic: {@topic} | rounds: {@max_rounds}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Opening statements
    GENERATE pro_argument(@topic, 'opening statement') INTO @pro
    GENERATE con_argument(@topic, 'opening statement') INTO @con

    @pro_history := @pro
    @con_history := @con

    LOGGING 'Opening statements complete' LEVEL INFO
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/opening_pro.md', @pro) INTO @_
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/opening_con.md', @con) INTO @_

    -- Rebuttal rounds
    WHILE @round < @max_rounds DO
        LOGGING f'Round {@round} | pro rebuttal ...' LEVEL DEBUG
        -- Pro rebuts Con's latest point
        GENERATE pro_argument(@topic, @con_history) INTO @pro_rebuttal
        @pro_history := @pro_history || '\n---\n' || @pro_rebuttal
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/round_{@round}_pro.md', @pro_rebuttal) INTO @_

        LOGGING f'Round {@round} | con rebuttal ...' LEVEL DEBUG
        -- Con rebuts Pro's latest point
        GENERATE con_argument(@topic, @pro_history) INTO @con_rebuttal
        @con_history := @con_history || '\n---\n' || @con_rebuttal
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/round_{@round}_con.md', @con_rebuttal) INTO @_

        @round := @round + 1
        LOGGING f'Round {@round} complete' LEVEL INFO
    END

    LOGGING 'All rounds done — judge deliberating ...' LEVEL INFO

    -- Judge evaluates both sides
    GENERATE judge_debate(@topic, @pro_history, @con_history) INTO @verdict

    LOGGING f'Verdict ready | rounds={@round}' LEVEL INFO
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/verdict.md', @verdict) INTO @_
    COMMIT @verdict WITH status = 'complete', rounds = @round

EXCEPTION
    WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
        GENERATE judge_debate(@topic, @pro_history, @con_history) INTO @verdict
        COMMIT @verdict WITH status = 'partial'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @pro_history WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```
     |

     | [INFO] Opening statements complete
     | [INFO] Round 1 complete
     | [INFO] Round 2 complete
     | [INFO] Round 3 complete
     | [INFO] All rounds done — judge deliberating ...
     | [INFO] Verdict ready | rounds=3
     | ============================================================
     | Caller:  momagrid-hub
     | Adapter: momagrid
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 9
     | Tokens: 9941 in / 3298 out
     | Latency: 71738ms
     | Timestamp: 2026-03-27 09:01:47
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output
Based on the evaluation, I declare the PRO side as the winner.

The PRO side presented stronger arguments against the risks associated with open-sourcing AI. They effectively addressed concerns about safety, security, and intellectual property rights by highlighting the need for robust governance frameworks and proprietary approaches to mitigate these risks. Their rebuttals were more effective in countering the CON side's claims, particularly in addressing the notion that open-sourcing AI would create an "unregulated Wild West scenario" where malicious actors could exploit vulnerable systems.

In contrast, the CON side's arguments, while well-intentioned, relied heavily on hypothetical scenarios and misunderstandings of the benefits of open-sourcing AI. They failed to adequately address the PRO side's points about transparency, accountability, and quality assurance, which are core principles of open-sourcing AI. Furthermore, their rebuttals often relied on unproven assumptions and overly simplistic solutions.

The PRO side's arguments were also more nuanced and comprehensive, taking into account the complexities of AI development and deployment. They presented a clear vision for how open-sourcing AI could be implemented in a way that balances innovation with safety and security concerns. Overall, while both sides presented valid points, I believe that the PRO side presented stronger arguments and rebuttals, making them the winners of this debate.
```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------

     |   @topic = AI should be open-sourced

     |   @pro_history = Ladies and gentlemen, esteemed judges, and fellow debaters, I am honored to present my strongest argument in favor of the motion: "AI should be open-sourced."
     | 
     | My opponent may have raised several concerns about the potential risks of open-sourcing AI, but I firmly believe that these risks far outweigh any benefits. Firstly, by making AI an open-source technology, we empower a community of developers and researchers to review, critique, and improve upon its code. This collaborative approach fosters transparency, accountability, and quality assurance, ensuring that AI systems are designed with human values and ethics in mind.
     | 
     | Furthermore, open-sourcing AI will accelerate innovation and progress. By allowing anyone to access and contribute to the development of AI, we tap into a vast pool of talent and expertise, driving the technology forward at an unprecedented pace. This is particularly crucial in today's rapidly evolving technological landscape, where AI has the potential to revolutionize industries and transform our lives. By embracing open-sourcing, we ensure that AI remains a tool for the greater good, rather than a luxury reserved for select few.
     | 
     | My opponent may argue that open-sourcing AI puts sensitive information at risk of being compromised or misused. I counter that this concern is overstated and easily mitigable. With proper security protocols and governance structures in place, we can safeguard the integrity of our AI systems while still reaping the benefits of open collaboration. In fact, many successful projects have already demonstrated the feasibility of secure, open-source development – take, for instance, the Linux operating system.
     | 
     | In conclusion, I firmly believe that open-sourcing AI is not only a moral imperative but also a strategic necessity. By embracing this approach, we can harness the collective genius of humanity to create more equitable, sustainable, and beneficial technologies.
     | ---
     | My opponent raises several concerns about open-sourcing AI, but their arguments are fundamentally flawed and misinformed.
     | 
     | Firstly, the notion that releasing AI into the wild without control or oversight would lead to catastrophic consequences is a gross exaggeration. The very opposite is true: openness and transparency can actually mitigate these risks. By sharing knowledge and expertise, researchers and developers can collaborate on identifying and mitigating potential vulnerabilities, creating a collective defense against malicious actors. In contrast, secrecy and exclusivity only serve to perpetuate the "ivory tower" mentality of isolated innovation, where problems are ignored or downplayed.
     | 
     | Moreover, my opponent's concern about intellectual property rights is misplaced. The open-source community has consistently demonstrated its ability to foster collaboration, innovation, and progress without sacrificing intellectual property protections. In fact, open-sourcing AI can actually increase the incentive for creators to develop better technologies, as they compete with others in the same arena. Furthermore, the benefits of open-sourcing AI far outweigh any perceived loss of IP rights: it enables the creation of new products, services, and industries that would not be possible under a proprietary regime.
     | 
     | Regarding the practical challenge of regulating such a complex system, my opponent's skepticism is misplaced. The development of governance frameworks for AI is an active area of research, with experts working tirelessly to create standards, guidelines, and regulations that balance innovation with safety and security. Open-sourcing AI can actually facilitate this process, as it provides a platform for researchers to share their work, collaborate on testing and validation, and contribute to the development of more robust regulatory frameworks.
     | 
     | In conclusion, the risks associated with open-sourcing AI are overstated, while its benefits are substantial. By embracing openness and transparency, we can accelerate innovation, foster collaboration, and create a safer, more secure technological landscape for all. We must not be afraid to challenge the status quo and explore new possibilities – after all, it is only through open dialogue and debate that we will truly unlock the full potential of AI.
     | ---
     | To my opponent's claims that releasing AI into the wild without control or oversight would lead to catastrophic consequences, I say: the risks associated with open-sourcing AI far outweigh the benefits of unregulated collaboration. While it is true that malicious actors may exploit vulnerable systems, this is precisely why we need to develop and implement robust governance frameworks, not simply throw open the gates and hope for the best.
     | 
     | In reality, the lack of regulation and accountability would create a Wild West scenario where unscrupulous developers and nation-states engage in a free-for-all of exploitation and sabotage. But I propose that this is precisely why we should invest in developing and enforcing stricter regulations and standards for AI development and deployment. By doing so, we can ensure that the benefits of open-source collaboration are matched by corresponding safeguards and protections.
     | 
     | Furthermore, my opponent's assertion that intellectual property rights would be compromised by open-sourcing AI is a red herring designed to distract from the real issue at hand: promoting transparency and cooperation in AI development. The open-source community has consistently demonstrated its ability to foster innovation and progress through collaborative efforts, not by sacrificing IP protections. In fact, many open-source AI projects have already shown that they can thrive without compromising intellectual property rights.
     | 
     | By embracing openness and collaboration in AI development, we can accelerate the pace of innovation while minimizing the risks associated with unregulated technology. We don't need to throw open the gates and risk unleashing a Pandora's box of unmitigated risks; instead, we can develop and implement robust governance frameworks that prioritize safety, security, and responsible innovation. I urge you to join me in embracing the benefits of open-sourcing AI – not as a reckless gamble, but as a deliberate choice to harness its power for the greater good.
     | ---
     | My opponent's attempt to discredit open-sourcing AI by citing the risks of unregulated collaboration and exploitation is fundamentally flawed. They claim that releasing sensitive technology into the wild would lead to catastrophic consequences, but this is a classic case of fear-mongering without a solid foundation.
     | 
     | The notion that open-sourcing AI will create an "unregulated Wild West scenario" is a misconception. In reality, many open-source projects have proven themselves to be robust and secure, with thriving communities that contribute to the codebase and provide mutual support. The key to successful open-sourcing lies not in the absence of regulation, but in the presence of clear guidelines, standards, and governance frameworks. By establishing these frameworks, we can ensure that AI developers work together to identify and address potential vulnerabilities, rather than relying on a one-size-fits-all approach.
     | 
     | Furthermore, my opponent's assertion that open-sourcing AI compromises intellectual property rights is a red herring designed to distract from the real issue at hand: promoting transparency and cooperation in AI development. The reality is that many open-source projects have already established robust IP frameworks, which allow creators to maintain control over their work while still benefiting from the collaborative efforts of others. In fact, open-sourcing AI can often lead to faster innovation and greater progress, as developers are incentivized to contribute to a shared effort rather than hoarding knowledge for personal gain.
     | 
     | In contrast, my opponent's proposal relies on a paternalistic approach that assumes developers will magically self-regulate without clear guidelines or oversight. This is not only unrealistic but also undermines the autonomy of AI researchers and developers, who should be trusted to make informed decisions about their work. By open-sourcing AI, we empower these individuals to take ownership of their creations and work together to drive innovation forward, rather than relying on top-down approaches that stifle creativity and progress.
     | 
     | In conclusion, my opponent's arguments against open-sourcing AI are based on a flawed understanding of the technology and its potential benefits. By establishing clear guidelines, standards, and governance frameworks, we can harness the power of open-sourcing to drive innovation forward while ensuring safety, security, and intellectual property protections. I urge you to reject my opponent's motion and support the motion that AI should be open-sourced.
     |   @con_history = Ladies and gentlemen, honorable judges, and fellow debaters, I am here to present a compelling argument against the motion that AI should be open-sourced.

     | While the idea of openness and transparency in technology may seem appealing at first glance, it is crucial to consider the potential consequences of making advanced artificial intelligence (AI) open-source. By doing so, we would essentially be releasing a powerful tool into the wild without proper control or oversight, allowing it to fall into the wrong hands. The risk of AI-powered cyber attacks, job displacement, and other negative consequences would become exponentially more likely. In today's world where nations are already vying for technological superiority, open-sourcing AI could inadvertently empower adversaries, who could exploit this vulnerability to their advantage.

     | 
     | In contrast, my opponent's proposal relies on the mythical notion of "collective defense" against malicious actors, which is nothing more than a pipe dream. In reality, the lack of oversight and accountability will only lead to a Wild West scenario where unscrupulous developers and nation-states engage in a free-for-all of exploitation and sabotage. We cannot afford to gamble with the global security and safety that AI promises to bring. The risks associated with open-sourcing AI are too great, and we must prioritize caution and prudence over misguided notions of "openness" and "collaboration".
     | ---
     | My opponent's opening statement presents a flawed narrative about the benefits of open-sourcing AI, which I must debunk. In reality, releasing sensitive AI technology into the wild without proper vetting and review would inevitably lead to catastrophic consequences.      | 
     | Furthermore, my opponent's claim that open-sourcing AI accelerates innovation is misguided. In contrast, proprietary approaches ensure that research and development are driven by responsible innovation, prioritizing safety and security over short-term gains.
     | 
     | Moreover, my opponent's assertion that open-sourcing AI increases intellectual property rights is a red herring designed to distract from the real issue at hand: promoting transparency and cooperation in AI development. This would stifle innovation, as smaller entities struggle to compete with proprietary approaches.
     | 
     | In conclusion, my opponent's motion "AI should be open-sourced" is a recipe for disaster, prioritizing unregulated collaboration over safety and security. By doing so, we can ensure that the benefits of open-source collaboration are matched by corresponding safeguards and protections.

     | 
     | In contrast, my opponent's proposal relies on a paternalistic approach that assumes developers will magically self-regulate without clear guidelines or oversight. This is not only unrealistic but also undermines the autonomy of AI researchers and developers, who should be trusted to make informed decisions about their work. ```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 12: Plan and Execute
-- A planner agent decomposes a task into steps, then an executor implements each one.
-- When output_dir is set, generated code files are written to disk one-by-one.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/plan_execute.spl --adapter ollama \
--       --tools cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/tools.py \
--       task="Build a REST API for a todo app" \
--       output_dir="cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/output"
--
--   spl run cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/plan_execute.spl --adapter ollama \
--       task="Set up a CI/CD pipeline for a Python project"


-- Phase 1: Decompose task into a numbered implementation plan
CREATE FUNCTION plan(task TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
You are a senior software architect. Break down this task into clear implementation steps:

Task: {task}

Output a numbered list of concrete steps (3–6 steps). Each step should be specific
and independently executable. No prose — just the numbered list.
$$;

-- Count how many steps are in the plan (capped at max_steps)
CREATE FUNCTION count_steps(plan TEXT, max_steps TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
Count the number of numbered steps in this plan.
Return ONLY a single integer — no explanation, no text.
If there are more than {max_steps} steps, return {max_steps}.

Plan:
{plan}
$$;

-- Extract step N from the plan (0-indexed)
CREATE FUNCTION extract_step(plan TEXT, step_index TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
Extract step number {step_index} (0-indexed) from this numbered plan and return
ONLY that step's description. No numbering prefix. No extra text.

Plan:
{plan}
$$;

-- Execute a step: produce a concise design decision + list of files to create
CREATE FUNCTION execute_step(current_step TEXT, prior_results TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
You are a senior software engineer planning an implementation.

Step: {current_step}
Completed so far: {prior_results}

Describe in 2-3 sentences what this step produces and list the filenames it creates.
Be brief — no code yet, just the design decision and file list.
$$;

-- Validate that a step produced meaningful output (design notes, not code)
CREATE FUNCTION validate_step(current_step TEXT, step_result TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
Does the following design note meaningfully address the step?
Note: this is a planning phase — expect a 2-3 sentence description and a file list, NOT code.

Step: {current_step}
Design note: {step_result}

Reply with exactly one word: "passed" or "failed".
Only reply "failed" if the design note is empty, completely off-topic, or missing a file list.
$$;

-- Re-plan after a step failure
CREATE FUNCTION replan(task TEXT, plan TEXT, step_index TEXT, step_result TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
A step in our implementation plan failed. Create a revised plan.

Original task: {task}
Original plan: {plan}
Failed at step: {step_index}
Failure details: {step_result}

Output a new numbered plan starting from step {step_index}. Keep completed steps as-is.
$$;

-- Phase 4a: List all source files to create (no code — just filenames + one-line descriptions)
CREATE FUNCTION outline_files(task TEXT, results TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
You are a senior software engineer. Based on the implementation plan below, list every
source file that must be created to complete the task.

Task: {task}

Implementation plan:
{results}

Output a numbered list — one file per line — with the filename and a one-sentence description.
Always include a README.md as the last item.
Example:
1. app.py - Flask application entry point, registers blueprints and starts the server
2. models.py - SQLAlchemy ORM models for Todo and User
3. README.md - Setup instructions, project layout, and example API calls

No code. No prose. Just the numbered file list.
$$;

-- Count files in the outline
CREATE FUNCTION count_files(outline TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
Count the number of numbered items in this file list.
Return ONLY a single integer — no explanation, no text.

List:
{outline}
$$;

-- Extract one file entry at index N (0-indexed)
CREATE FUNCTION extract_file(outline TEXT, file_index TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
Extract item number {file_index} (0-indexed) from this numbered file list and return
ONLY that item's filename and description. No number prefix. No extra text.

List:
{outline}
$$;

-- Generate complete code for ONE file
CREATE FUNCTION generate_file(task TEXT, results TEXT, file_desc TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
You are a senior software engineer. Write complete, runnable code for ONE source file.

Task: {task}

Implementation plan:
{results}

File to create: {file_desc}

Output a SINGLE fenced code block. The FIRST line inside the fence MUST be a filename comment:

```python
# filename: app/main.py
...complete code...
```
     | 
     | Requirements:
     | - Complete, runnable code — no placeholders, no TODOs.
     | - Error handling, type hints, brief inline comments.
     | - Only this one file — nothing else.
     | $$;
     | 
     | -- Summarize what was built
     | CREATE FUNCTION summarize(task TEXT, results TEXT, file_outline TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
     | AS $$
     | In 3-5 sentences, describe what was built for this task and how to run it.
     | 
     | Task: {task}
     | Files created:
     | {file_outline}
     | $$;
     | 
     | 
     | WORKFLOW plan_and_execute
     |     INPUT:
     |         @task TEXT DEFAULT 'Build a REST API for a todo app',
     |         @output_dir TEXT DEFAULT '',
     |         @max_steps INTEGER DEFAULT 5,
     |         @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/logs'
     |     OUTPUT: @final_report TEXT
     | DO
     |     @step_index := 0
     |     @results := ''
     |     @replan_count := 0
     |     @max_replans := 3
     | 
     |     LOGGING f'Plan-and-Execute | task: {@task}' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Phase 1: Plan
     |     GENERATE plan(@task) INTO @plan
     |     LOGGING f'Plan ready | steps to execute (max={@max_steps})' LEVEL INFO
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/plan.md', @plan) INTO @_
     | 
     |     -- Phase 2: Count steps (capped at max_steps)
     |     GENERATE count_steps(@plan, @max_steps) INTO @step_count
     |     LOGGING f'Step count: {@step_count}' LEVEL DEBUG
     | 
     |     -- Phase 3: Execute each step (brief design notes only — no code)
     |     WHILE @step_index < @step_count DO
     |         LOGGING f'Executing step {@step_index}/{@step_count} ...' LEVEL INFO
     |         GENERATE extract_step(@plan, @step_index) INTO @current_step
     |         GENERATE execute_step(@current_step, @results) INTO @step_result
     |         GENERATE validate_step(@current_step, @step_result) INTO @validation
     | 
     |         EVALUATE @validation
     |             WHEN 'failed' THEN
     |                 @replan_count := @replan_count + 1
     |                 LOGGING f'Step {@step_index} failed — replanning ({@replan_count}/{@max_replans})' LEVEL WARN
     |                 EVALUATE @replan_count
     |                     WHEN >= @max_replans THEN
     |                         LOGGING f'Max replans reached — accepting step as-is' LEVEL WARN
     |                         @results := @results || '\n## Step ' || @step_index || '\n' || @step_result
     |                         CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/step_{@step_index}.md', @step_result) INTO @_
     |                         @step_index := @step_index + 1
     |                     ELSE
     |                         GENERATE replan(@task, @plan, @step_index, @step_result) INTO @plan
     |                         GENERATE count_steps(@plan, @max_steps) INTO @step_count
     |                         @step_index := 0
     |                         @results := ''
     |                 END
     |             ELSE
     |                 @results := @results || '\n## Step ' || @step_index || '\n' || @step_result
     |                 CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/step_{@step_index}.md', @step_result) INTO @_
     |                 @step_index := @step_index + 1
     |                 LOGGING f'Step {@step_index} done' LEVEL DEBUG
     |         END
     |     END
     | 
     |     LOGGING f'All {@step_count} steps complete — generating files' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Phase 4: Outline all files to generate
     |     GENERATE outline_files(@task, @results) INTO @file_outline
     |     GENERATE count_files(@file_outline) INTO @file_count
     |     LOGGING f'File outline ready | {@file_count} files to generate' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Phase 5: Generate each file individually (one LLM call per file — avoids truncation)
     |     @file_index := 0
     |     WHILE @file_index < @file_count DO
     |         LOGGING f'Generating file {@file_index}/{@file_count} ...' LEVEL INFO
     |         GENERATE extract_file(@file_outline, @file_index) INTO @current_file
     |         GENERATE generate_file(@task, @results, @current_file) INTO @file_code
     |         CALL write_code_files(@file_code, @output_dir) INTO @file_written
     |         LOGGING f'Written: {@file_written}' LEVEL DEBUG
     |         @file_index := @file_index + 1
     |     END
     | 
     |     LOGGING f'All {@file_count} files generated' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Phase 6: Summary report
     |     GENERATE summarize(@task, @results, @file_outline) INTO @final_report
     | 
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_report.md', @final_report) INTO @_
     |     COMMIT @final_report WITH status = 'complete', steps_executed = @step_index, files_generated = @file_count
     | 
     | EXCEPTION
     |     WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
     |         GENERATE summarize(@task, @results, @file_outline) INTO @final_report
     |         COMMIT @final_report WITH status = 'partial', steps_executed = @step_index
     |     WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
     |         COMMIT @results WITH status = 'budget_limit'
     | END
     | ```
     | 
     | [INFO] Plan-and-Execute | task: Build a REST API for a todo app
     | [INFO] Plan ready | steps to execute (max=5)
     | [INFO] Executing step 0/6 ...
     | [WARN] Step 0 failed — replanning (1/3)
     | [INFO] Executing step 0/5 ...
     | [INFO] Executing step 1/5 ...
     | [WARN] Step 1 failed — replanning (2/3)
     | [INFO] Executing step 0/5 ...
     | [WARN] Step 0 failed — replanning (3/3)
     | [WARN] Max replans reached — accepting step as-is
     | [INFO] Executing step 1/5 ...
     | ============================================================
     | Caller:  momagrid-hub
     | Adapter: momagrid
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 25
     | Tokens: 6025 in / 1520 out
     | Latency: 73784ms
     | Timestamp: 2026-03-27 09:03:01
     | ------------------------------------------------------------

## Step 0

```spl
-- Recipe 13: Map-Reduce Summarizer
-- Splits a large document into chunks, summarizes each chunk (map),
-- then combines all summaries into a final summary (reduce).
-- Demonstrates the map-reduce pattern for handling content beyond context limits.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --tools cookbook/13_map_reduce/tools.py --adapter ollama -m gemma3 document="$(cat large_doc.txt)"
--   spl run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --tools cookbook/13_map_reduce/tools.py --adapter ollama document="$(cat README.md)" style="bullet points"
--   spl run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --tools cookbook/13_map_reduce/tools.py --adapter ollama document="long text here..." style="executive brief"
--
-- Tools: chunk_plan(), extract_chunk() are in tools.py (CALL, zero tokens).
--        write_file(), read_file() are SPL built-ins — no --tools flag needed.
--        summarize_chunk(), reduce_summaries(), quality_score(), improve_summary() call the LLM.
--
-- Logs written to @log_dir (default: cookbook/13_map_reduce/logs):
--   chunk_00.md, chunk_01.md, ...    — raw extracted chunks
--   summary_00.md, summary_01.md, ... — per-chunk LLM summaries
--   final_summary.md                  — combined final summary

WORKFLOW map_reduce_summarizer
    INPUT:  
        @document TEXT, 
        @style TEXT,
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/13_map_reduce/logs'
    OUTPUT: @final_summary TEXT
DO
    @chunk_index := 0
    @summaries := []          -- LIST: accumulates per-chunk summaries

    LOGGING 'Starting map-reduce | document length: ' + @document

    -- Step 1: Determine how to split the document (deterministic, 0 tokens)
    CALL chunk_plan(@document) INTO @chunk_count

    LOGGING 'Document split into ' + @chunk_count + ' chunks'

    -- Step 2: MAP — summarize each chunk independently
    WHILE @chunk_index < @chunk_count DO
        CALL extract_chunk(@document, @chunk_index, @chunk_count) INTO @chunk   -- deterministic, 0 tokens

        -- Save raw chunk to logs/
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/chunk_{@chunk_index}.md', @chunk) INTO @_

        GENERATE summarize_chunk(@chunk, @chunk_index) INTO @chunk_summary

        -- Save chunk summary to logs/
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/summary_{@chunk_index}.md', @chunk_summary) INTO @_

        LOGGING f'[Chunk {@chunk_index}/{@chunk_count}] summary saved'

        @summaries := list_append(@summaries, @chunk_summary)
        @chunk_index := @chunk_index + 1
    END

    -- Step 3: REDUCE — concat all chunk summaries, then combine into final
    @summaries_text := list_concat(@summaries, '\n')
    GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries_text, @style) INTO @final_summary

    -- Step 4: Quality check the final summary
    GENERATE quality_score(@final_summary, @document) INTO @score

    EVALUATE @score
        WHEN > 0.7 THEN
            CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_summary.md', @final_summary) INTO @_
            LOGGING f'Final summary saved to {@log_dir}/final_summary.md (score={@score})'
            COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'complete', chunks = @chunk_count
        ELSE
            GENERATE improve_summary(@final_summary, @summaries_text) INTO @final_summary
            CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_summary.md', @final_summary) INTO @_
            LOGGING f'Improved summary saved to {@log_dir}/final_summary.md (score={@score})'
            COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'refined', chunks = @chunk_count
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        @summaries_text := list_concat(@summaries, '\n')
        GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries_text, @style) INTO @final_summary
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_summary.md', @final_summary) INTO @_
        COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'partial'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        @summaries_text := list_concat(@summaries, '\n')
        GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries_text, @style) INTO @final_summary
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_summary.md', @final_summary) INTO @_
        COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     | [INFO] [Chunk 0/1] summary saved
     | [INFO] Improved summary saved to cookbook/13_map_reduce/logs/final_summary.md (score=### Quality Score Function
     | This function calculates the quality score of a summary based on its similarity with other summaries in the dataset.

     | #### Code
```python
import re
from difflib import SequenceMatcher

def reduce_summaries(summaries):
    """
    Reduces a list of summaries to their most similar counterparts.
    
    Args:
    summaries (list): A list of summaries to be reduced.
    
    Returns:
    list: A list of tuples containing the original summary and its most similar counterpart, along with their similarity ratio.
    """

    # Initialize an empty list to store the most similar summaries
    similar_summaries = []

    # Iterate over each summary in the list
    for i, summary in enumerate(summaries):
        # Calculate the similarity ratio with all other summaries
        ratios = [SequenceMatcher(None, summary, s).ratio() for j, s in enumerate(summaries) if j != i]

        # Get the index of the most similar summary (excluding itself)
        most_similar_index = max(range(len(ratios)), key=lambda k: ratios[k])

        # If a similar summary is found, add it to the list
        if most_similar_index != i:
            similar_summaries.append((summary, summaries[most_similar_index]))

    # Return the most similar summaries with their similarity ratio
    return [(s[0], s[1], s[2]) for s in similar_summaries]

# Test the function
summarize_chunk = "The quick brown fox jumps over the lazy dog."
summarize_chunk_2 = "This is a test document for map-reduce summarization."

result = reduce_summaries([summarize_chunk, summarize_chunk_2])
print(result)
```
     | 
     | #### Explanation
     | 
     | *   The function `reduce_summaries` takes a list of summaries as input and returns a list of tuples containing the original summary and its most similar counterpart along with their similarity ratio.
     | *   It iterates over each summary in the list, calculates the similarity ratio with all other summaries using the SequenceMatcher from difflib library, and finds the index of the most similar summary (excluding itself).
     | *   If a similar summary is found, it is added to the `similar_summaries` list along with its similarity ratio.
     | *   Finally, the function returns the `similar_summaries` list containing the original summaries and their most similar counterparts along with their similarity ratios.
     | 
     | #### Example Use Case
     | 
     | *   The example usage demonstrates how to use the `reduce_summaries` function by passing a list of two summaries (`summarize_chunk` and `summarize_chunk_2`) as input.
     | *   The output will be a list of tuples containing the original summaries and their most similar counterparts along with their similarity ratios.)

Here is an improved version of your code:

```python
import re
from difflib import SequenceMatcher
from typing import List, Tuple

def reduce_summaries(summarize_chunk: str, other_text: str) -> List[Tuple[str, str]]:
    """
    Reduces a list of summaries to the most similar ones.

    Args:
        summarize_chunk (str): The original text that needs to be summarized.
        other_text (str): Additional context for summarization.

    Returns:
        List[Tuple[str, str]]: A list of tuples containing the most similar summary and its similarity ratio with another summary or the input text.
    """
    
    # Initialize an empty list to store the most similar summaries
    similar_summaries = []
    max_similarity_ratio = 0
    
    # Iterate over each possible split position in the summarize_chunk
    for i in range(1, len(summarize_chunk)):
        # Extract the summary chunk
        summary = summarize_chunk[:i]
        
        # Calculate the similarity ratio with other_text
        ratios = [SequenceMatcher(None, summary, s).ratio() for j, s in enumerate(other_text.split()) if s != '']
        
        # Get the index of the most similar text (excluding empty strings)
        most_similar_index = max(range(len(ratios)), key=lambda k: ratios[k] + 0.5)  # Add a small value to handle ties
        
        # If a similar summary is found, add it to the list
        if most_similar_index != -1 and ratios[most_similar_index] > max_similarity_ratio:
            max_similarity_ratio = ratios[most_similar_index]
            similar_summaries.append((summary, f"Other text: {other_text.split()[most_similar_index]}"))
    
    # Return the most similar summaries with their similarity ratio
    return [(s[0], s[1]) for s in similar_summaries]

# Test the function
summarize_chunk = "The quick brown fox jumps over the lazy dog."
other_text = bullet points
result = reduce_summaries(summarize_chunk, other_text)
print(result)
```

     | -  Changed how similarity ratios are calculated. Instead of comparing each summary with all others, we now compare each summary with every word in the other text. This allows us to find more meaningful summaries.
     | -  Made the calculation of `most_similar_index` slightly more complex by adding a small value to handle ties. This ensures that we choose the most similar summary even when multiple summaries have the same similarity ratio.
     | -  Modified the print statement to include the full text instead of just "Other text: [word]".

```python
import re
from difflib import SequenceMatcher

def reduce_summaries(summaries):
    """
    Reduces a list of summaries to their most similar counterparts.
    
    Args:
    summaries (list): A list of summaries to be reduced.
    
    Returns:
    list: A list of tuples containing the original summary and its most similar counterpart, along with their similarity ratio.
    """

    # Initialize an empty list to store the most similar summaries
    similar_summaries = []

    # Iterate over each summary in the list
    for i, summary in enumerate(summaries):
        # Calculate the similarity ratio with all other summaries
        ratios = [SequenceMatcher(None, summary, s).ratio() for j, s in enumerate(summaries) if j != i]

        # Get the index of the most similar summary (excluding itself)
        most_similar_index = max(range(len(ratios)), key=lambda k: ratios[k])

        # If a similar summary is found, add it to the list
        if most_similar_index != i:
            similar_summaries.append((summary, summaries[most_similar_index]))

    # Return the most similar summaries with their similarity ratio
    return [(s[0], s[1], s[2]) for s in similar_summaries]

# Test the function
summarize_chunk = "The quick brown fox jumps over the lazy dog."
summarize_chunk_2 = "This is a test document for map-reduce summarization."

result = reduce_summaries([summarize_chunk, summarize_chunk_2])
print(result)

```spl
-- Recipe 14: Multi-Agent Collaboration
-- Specialized agents (Researcher, Analyst, Writer) collaborate on a report.
-- Each agent has a distinct role and passes results to the next.
-- Demonstrates procedural decomposition, CALL-based delegation, and role specialization.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="Impact of AI on healthcare"
--   spl run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama topic="Future of renewable energy"
--   spl run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama topic="State of quantum computing in 2025"
--
-- Logs written to @log_dir (default: cookbook/14_multi_agent/logs):
--   research.md   — facts and key themes from Researcher agent
--   analysis.md   — trends, risks, and opportunities from Analyst agent
--   report.md     — final polished report from Writer agent

-- Agent 1: Researcher — gathers key facts and sources
PROCEDURE researcher(topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE research_facts(topic) INTO @facts
    GENERATE identify_key_themes(@facts) INTO @themes
    @result := @facts || '\n\nKey Themes:\n' || @themes
    COMMIT @result
END

-- Agent 2: Analyst — identifies trends, risks, and opportunities
PROCEDURE analyst(research TEXT, topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE analyze_trends(research) INTO @trends
    GENERATE assess_risks(research, topic) INTO @risks
    GENERATE find_opportunities(research, topic) INTO @opportunities
    @result := 'Trends:\n' || @trends || '\n\nRisks:\n' || @risks || '\n\nOpportunities:\n' || @opportunities
    COMMIT @result
END

-- Agent 3: Writer — produces polished final report
PROCEDURE writer(research TEXT, analysis TEXT, topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE draft_report(topic, research, analysis) INTO @draft
    GENERATE critique(@draft) INTO @feedback
    GENERATE revise_report(@draft, @feedback) INTO @final
    COMMIT @final
END

-- Orchestrator workflow
WORKFLOW multi_agent_report
    INPUT: 
        @topic TEXT,
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/14_multi_agent/logs'
    OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Multi-agent report | topic={@topic}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Delegate to specialized agents
    LOGGING 'Agent 1: Researcher ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL researcher(@topic) INTO @research
    LOGGING f'Research:\n{@research}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/research.md', @research) INTO @_
    LOGGING 'Agent 2: Analyst ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL analyst(@research, @topic) INTO @analysis
    LOGGING f'Analysis:\n{@analysis}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/analysis.md', @analysis) INTO @_
    LOGGING 'Agent 3: Writer ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL writer(@research, @analysis, @topic) INTO @report
    LOGGING f'Report:\n{@report}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/report.md', @report) INTO @_

    LOGGING 'Report complete' LEVEL INFO
    COMMIT @report WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @research WITH status = 'partial_research_only'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1 LIMIT 3
END
```

Here is a revised version of the report based on the feedback provided:

**The Impact of Artificial Intelligence (AI) on Healthcare: A Comprehensive Analysis**

**Executive Summary**

Artificial Intelligence (AI) is transforming the healthcare industry by improving patient outcomes, increasing efficiency, and enhancing patient engagement. This report provides an in-depth analysis of the impact of AI on healthcare, highlighting its potential benefits, uses, and challenges.

**Introduction**

The integration of Artificial Intelligence (AI) into healthcare has been gaining momentum in recent years. The use of AI-powered systems is revolutionizing the way healthcare professionals diagnose, treat, and manage diseases. However, there are also concerns about bias, job displacement, and cybersecurity risks associated with AI in healthcare. This report aims to provide a comprehensive overview of the impact of AI on healthcare, exploring its potential benefits, challenges, and opportunities.

**Key Benefits**

1. **Improved Patient Outcomes**: AI algorithms can analyze medical images, genetic profiles, and lifestyle data to improve diagnosis accuracy, predict patient outcomes, and personalize treatment plans.
2. **Increased Efficiency**: AI-powered systems can automate routine administrative tasks, streamline clinical workflows, and reduce the burden on healthcare professionals.
3. **Enhanced Patient Engagement**: AI-powered virtual assistants, personalized messaging, and appointment reminders can help patients manage their health, track medication adherence, and engage in healthier behaviors.

**Key Applications**

1. **Medical Imaging Analysis**: AI can analyze medical images such as X-rays, CT scans, and MRI scans to detect diseases earlier and more accurately.
2. **Predictive Analytics**: AI-powered predictive analytics can identify high-risk patients, predict patient outcomes, and help healthcare professionals make informed decisions.
3. **Clinical Decision Support**: AI-powered clinical decision support systems can provide real-time insights and recommendations to inform diagnoses and treatment plans.

**Challenges and Limitations**

1. **Bias in Decision-Making**: AI systems can perpetuate biases present in the data used to train them, leading to biased diagnoses or treatment recommendations.
2. **Data Security Breaches**: The increased reliance on AI-powered systems may also increase the risk of cyber attacks and data breaches, compromising patient confidentiality and security.
3. **Job Displacement**: AI-powered automation could lead to job displacement for healthcare professionals, particularly in administrative tasks.

**Opportunities**

1. **Personalized Medicine**: AI can help personalize treatment plans by analyzing a patient's genetic profile, medical history, and lifestyle data.
2. **Streamlined Clinical Decision-Making**: AI-powered clinical decision support systems can provide real-time insights and recommendations to inform diagnoses and treatment plans.
3. **Accelerating Medical Research**: AI can help analyze vast amounts of medical data to identify potential new treatments, therapies, and breakthroughs in medical research.

**Mitigation Strategies**

1. **Regular Auditing and Testing**: Regularly auditing and testing AI systems to identify biases, errors, or areas for improvement.
2. **Data Governance**: Implementing robust data governance policies to ensure data quality, security, and transparency.
3. **Transparency and Explainability**: Developing transparent and explainable AI systems to facilitate understanding and trust among healthcare professionals and patients.

**Conclusion**

The impact of Artificial Intelligence (AI) on healthcare is complex, with both benefits and challenges. While AI has the potential to improve patient outcomes, increase efficiency, and enhance patient engagement, there are also concerns about bias, job displacement, and data security breaches. To maximize the benefits of AI in healthcare while minimizing its negative consequences, it is essential to address these challenges through regular auditing and testing, robust data governance policies, and transparency and explainability.

**Recommendations**

1. **Invest in AI Research and Development**: Invest in research and development to improve the accuracy and effectiveness of AI-powered systems in healthcare.
2. **Implement Robust Data Governance Policies**: Implement robust data governance policies to ensure data quality, security, and transparency.
3. **Develop Transparent and Explainable AI Systems**: Develop transparent and explainable AI systems to facilitate understanding and trust among healthcare professionals and patients.

**Final Thoughts**

The impact of Artificial Intelligence on healthcare is a complex and multifaceted topic. This report provides an in-depth analysis of the benefits, challenges, and opportunities associated with AI in healthcare. By acknowledging both the advantages and disadvantages of AI in healthcare, we can work towards harnessing its potential to improve patient outcomes, increase efficiency, and enhance patient engagement.

Some key changes made include:

* Added more context for readers who are new to the topic
* Delved deeper into potential drawbacks, limitations, and controversies surrounding AI in healthcare
* Strengthened conclusion to provide a more nuanced summary of the report's findings
* Included more data-driven insights and statistics to support arguments
* Provided actionable recommendations for policymakers, healthcare professionals, and researchers

     | 
     | 1. **Improved Diagnosis Accuracy**: AI algorithms can analyze medical images, such as X-rays and MRIs, to help doctors diagnose diseases more accurately and quickly.
     | 
     | 2. **Personalized Medicine**: AI can help personalize treatment plans by analyzing a patient's genetic profile, medical history, and lifestyle data.

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 15: Automated Code Review
-- Multi-pass code review: security audit, performance check, style review, then synthesis.
-- Demonstrates tool-augmented analysis, multi-criteria evaluation, and structured output.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 code="$(cat myfile.py)"
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="$(cat main.go)" language="Go"
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="def foo(x): return eval(x)" language="Python"
--
-- Logs written to @log_dir (default: cookbook/15_code_review/logs):
--   security.md    — security audit findings
--   performance.md — performance analysis findings
--   style.md       — style and best-practice findings
--   bugs.md        — bug detection findings
--   review.md      — final synthesized review

WORKFLOW code_review
    INPUT:
        @code     TEXT,
        @language TEXT,
        @log_dir  TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/15_code_review/logs'
    OUTPUT: @review TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Code review | language={@language}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Pass 1: Security audit
    GENERATE security_audit(@code, @language) INTO @security_findings
    LOGGING f'Security findings:\n{@security_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/security.md', @security_findings) INTO @_

    -- Pass 2: Performance analysis
    GENERATE performance_review(@code, @language) INTO @perf_findings
    LOGGING f'Performance findings:\n{@perf_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/performance.md', @perf_findings) INTO @_

    -- Pass 3: Code style and best practices
    GENERATE style_review(@code, @language) INTO @style_findings
    LOGGING f'Style findings:\n{@style_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/style.md', @style_findings) INTO @_

    -- Pass 4: Bug detection
    GENERATE bug_detection(@code, @language) INTO @bug_findings
    LOGGING f'Bug findings:\n{@bug_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/bugs.md', @bug_findings) INTO @_

    -- Severity scoring for each category
    GENERATE severity_score(@security_findings) INTO @sec_score
    GENERATE severity_score(@perf_findings) INTO @perf_score
    GENERATE severity_score(@bug_findings) INTO @bug_score
    LOGGING f'Scores | sec={@sec_score} perf={@perf_score} bug={@bug_score}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Synthesize all findings into a structured review
    GENERATE synthesize_review(
        @security_findings, @sec_score,
        @perf_findings, @perf_score,
        @style_findings,
        @bug_findings, @bug_score
    ) INTO @review
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/review.md', @review) INTO @_

    -- Determine overall verdict
    EVALUATE @sec_score
        WHEN > 8 THEN
            LOGGING f'Critical security issues | score={@sec_score}' LEVEL WARN
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'critical_issues', verdict = 'block'
        WHEN > 5 THEN
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'needs_fixes', verdict = 'request_changes'
        ELSE
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'approved', verdict = 'approve'
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- Code too large — review in chunks
        GENERATE summarize_code(@code) INTO @summary
        GENERATE quick_review(@summary, @language) INTO @review
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/review.md', @review) INTO @_
        COMMIT @review WITH status = 'partial_large_file'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/security.md', @security_findings) INTO @_
        COMMIT @security_findings WITH status = 'security_only'
END
```

# Synthesizing Reviews for Security Audits

This guide provides a comprehensive overview of synthesizing reviews for security audits, including input and output examples. The aim is to provide actionable insights for improving code quality and mitigating security risks.

## Input 1: Security Audit Report

### Vulnerability Description

The `foo` function uses the `eval()` function to evaluate a string as Python code. This makes it vulnerable to code injection attacks, where an attacker can inject malicious code by crafting a specific input.

### Impact

An attacker could use this vulnerability to execute arbitrary code on the system, potentially leading to data theft, modification, or deletion, and even take control of the system.

### Recommendations

1.  Use a safe evaluation method: Instead of using `eval()`, consider using a safer evaluation method such as `ast.literal_eval()` or `safe_eval()`.
2.  Validate user input: Always validate and sanitize user input to prevent malicious code from being injected.
3.  Avoid using `eval()`: Consider rewriting the function to avoid using `eval()` altogether.

### Example of Safe Usage

```python
import ast

def foo(x):
    try:
        return ast.literal_eval(x)
    except (ValueError, TypeError):
        return "Invalid input"
```
     | 
     | In this example, we use `ast.literal_eval()` to safely evaluate the input string. If the input is not a valid Python literal, it raises an exception that we catch and return an error message.
     | 
     | ### Example of Sanitizing User Input
     | 
```python
def foo(x):
    x = str(x).strip()  # Remove leading/trailing whitespace
    if not x:  # Check for empty string
        raise ValueError("Invalid input")
    try:
        result = eval(x)
        return result
    except Exception as e:
        return f"Error: {e}"
```
     | 
     | In this example, we sanitize the user input by removing leading/trailing whitespace and checking for an empty string. We then use `eval()` to evaluate the sanitized input, but catch any exceptions that occur and return an error message.
     |

     | 2.  **Denial of Service (DoS)**: Malicious input can cause the interpreter to crash or enter an infinite loop.
     | 
     | ### Best Practices
     | 
     | To improve performance and security:
     | 
     | 1.  Use Safe Evaluation: Instead of using `eval`, consider using safer alternatives like `ast.literal_eval` for evaluating literals only.
     | 2.  Input Validation: Always validate user input before passing it to `eval`.
     | 3.  Avoid Evaluating User Input: Try to avoid using `eval` whenever possible and instead use a controlled, predefined set of functions.

     | 
```python
import ast

def safe_eval(x):
    try:
        return ast.literal_eval(x)
    except (ValueError, TypeError):
        raise ValueError("Invalid input")

# Usage:

result = safe_eval(user_input)
print(result)  # Output: 10
```
     |

     | 
     | While `eval` can be useful in some situations, it's essential to weigh the benefits against the potential security risks. By using safer alternatives and implementing proper input validation, you can minimize the risks associated with `eval`.

```python
import ast

def foo(x):
    try:
        return ast.literal_eval(x)
    except ValueError:
        raise ValueError("Invalid input")
```
     | 
     | In this version, we're using `ast.literal_eval()` to parse the input data. If the input is not a valid Python literal, it raises a `ValueError`.
     | 
     | **Best Practice:**
     | 
     | Consider using safer alternatives like string formatting or dedicated parsing libraries instead of `eval()`. For example, if you need to evaluate an expression, consider using the `numbers` module's `eval_expr()` function.
     |

import numbers

def foo(x):
    try:
        return numbers.eval_expr(str(x))
    except Exception as e:
        raise ValueError("Invalid input") from e
```

     |   @bug_findings = **Bug Detection and Fix**
     | 
     | The given code has a potential security vulnerability. The `eval()` function can be used to execute arbitrary Python code, which makes it a significant security risk.
     | 
     | ### Vulnerability
     | 
     | The issue with the current implementation is that it directly evaluates user-inputted strings as Python code. This allows an attacker to potentially inject malicious code.
     | 
     | ### Fix
     | 
     | To fix this vulnerability, we should avoid using `eval()` and instead opt for safer alternatives. Here's an updated version of the function that uses a safe evaluation method:
     | 
```python
def foo(x):
    try:
        # Use eval() only if x is a string literal (i.e., it doesn't contain any variables or functions)
        if isinstance(x, str) and "'" in x and '"' not in x:
            return eval(x)
        else:
            raise ValueError("Invalid input format")
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"Error: {e}")
        return None
```
     | 
     | However, for more complex calculations, consider using `numexpr` or `asteval`, which are safer alternatives:
     | 
```python
import numexpr as ne

def foo(x):
    try:
        # Use numexpr() to evaluate the expression
        result = ne.evaluate(x)
        return result
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"Error: {e}")
        return None
```
     | 
     | ### Best Practice
     | 
     | In general, it's recommended to avoid using `eval()` whenever possible. Instead, opt for safer alternatives like:
     | 
     | *   `numexpr` for simple arithmetic operations
     | *   `asteval` for safe evaluation of mathematical expressions with variables
     | *   Regular expression matching or other string processing methods for more complex logic
     | 
     | ### Example Use Case
     | 
```python
print(foo("1 + 2 * 3"))  # Output: 7
```
     | 
     | In this example, the input string `"1 + 2 * 3"` is safely evaluated using `numexpr()`, producing the expected result of `7`.
