     |     score = 0
     |     
     |     # Evaluate the input based on best practices for safe evaluation
     |     if isinstance(input, str):
     |         try:
     |             result = ast.literal_eval(input)
     |         except (ValueError, TypeError):
     |             raise ValueError("Invalid input")
     |         
     |         # If the score is below a certain threshold, increment it by 1
     |         if score < 5 and isinstance(result, int) or isinstance(result, float):
     |             score += 1
     |     
     |     # Return the final score
     |     return score
     | 
     | # Example usage:
     | user_input = "10"
     | print(severity_score())  # Output: 1

```spl
-- Recipe 16: Reflection Agent
-- The agent solves a problem, then reflects on its own reasoning to catch errors.
-- If reflection finds issues, it corrects and re-reflects until confident.
-- Demonstrates meta-cognitive loops and self-assessment patterns.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 problem="What are the trade-offs of microservices vs monoliths?"
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama problem="Design a URL shortener system"
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama problem="Explain why bubble sort is O(n^2)"

WORKFLOW reflection_agent
    INPUT:
        @problem TEXT,
        @max_reflections Number DEFAULT 3,
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/16_reflection/logs'
    OUTPUT: @answer TEXT
DO
    @iteration := 0
    @confidence := 0

    LOGGING f'Reflection agent started | max_reflections={@max_reflections}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Initial attempt at solving the problem
    GENERATE solve(@problem) INTO @answer
    LOGGING 'Initial solution ready' LEVEL INFO
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/answer_0.md', @answer) INTO @_

    -- Reflection loop: assess and improve
    WHILE @iteration < @max_reflections DO
        LOGGING f'Reflection iteration {@iteration} ...' LEVEL DEBUG
        -- Reflect: identify errors, gaps, or weak reasoning
        GENERATE reflect(@problem, @answer) INTO @reflection
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/reflection_{@iteration}.md', @reflection) INTO @_

        -- Score confidence in the answer
        GENERATE confidence_score(@answer, @reflection) INTO @confidence
        LOGGING f'Confidence score: {@confidence} | iteration={@iteration}' LEVEL DEBUG

        EVALUATE @confidence
            WHEN > 0.85 THEN
                LOGGING f'Confident at iteration {@iteration} | score={@confidence}' LEVEL INFO
                CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @answer) INTO @_
                COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'confident', confidence = @confidence, reflections = @iteration
            ELSE
                -- Extract specific issues to fix
                GENERATE extract_issues(@reflection) INTO @issues

                -- Correct the answer based on identified issues
                GENERATE correct(@answer, @issues, @problem) INTO @answer
                @iteration := @iteration + 1
                CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/answer_{@iteration}.md', @answer) INTO @_
        END
    END

    -- If loop exhausted, commit best effort with final confidence
    LOGGING f'Max reflections reached | confidence={@confidence}' LEVEL WARN
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @answer) INTO @_
    COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'best_effort', confidence = @confidence, reflections = @iteration

EXCEPTION
    WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @answer) INTO @_
        COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'max_reflections', confidence = @confidence
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        GENERATE solve(@problem) INTO @answer
        COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'restarted'
END
```

```output
To design a URL shortener system, I'll follow these steps:

1. Define the requirements and features of the system.

2. Choose an appropriate architecture (e.g., monolithic, microservices, event-driven).

3. Select technologies for the frontend and backend.

4. Design the database schema.

5. Implement API endpoints.

6. Develop a hash algorithm to generate unique shortened URLs.

7. Implement security measures such as validation, authentication, and data encryption.

8. Optimize performance using caching, load balancing, and efficient algorithms.

9. Monitor and maintain the system.


Here's an updated design following these steps:

**Overview**

The URL shortener system will take a long URL as input, store it in a database, and return a shortened version of the URL. The system will also keep track of the number of clicks on each shortened URL.

**Components**

1. **Frontend**: Handles user requests to shorten URLs (e.g., React, Angular)
2. **Backend**: Stores the mapping between shortened URLs and their corresponding long URLs in a database, and keeps track of the click count for each shortened URL using Redis's Pub/Sub mechanism (e.g., Node.js, Express)
3. **Database**: Stores the mapping between shortened URLs and long URLs, along with metadata such as created_at and updated_at timestamps
4. **Hash Algorithm**: Used to generate unique shortened URLs

**Design Pattern: API Gateway with Service-Oriented Architecture (SOA)**

The system will use an API gateway to handle incoming requests, which will delegate tasks to various services:

1. **URL Shortener Service**: Handles requests to shorten URLs and generates shortened URLs
2. **Database Service**: Stores and retrieves data from the database, using caching to improve performance

**API Endpoints**

The system will have two API endpoints:

1. **/shorten**: Accepts a long URL as input, generates a shortened URL, and returns it in JSON format.
2. **/click**: Takes a shortened URL as input, increments its click count using Redis's Pub/Sub mechanism, and updates the associated metadata.

**Hash Algorithm**

The system will use a custom hash algorithm to generate unique shortened URLs. The algorithm should be fast and efficient, and produce a consistent output for each input.

**Security Considerations**

1. **Validation**: Validate user input to prevent malicious requests.
2. **Authentication**: Authenticate users before allowing them to access the API.
3. **IP Address Logging**: Store IP addresses of users who click on shortened URLs for analytics purposes only.
4. **Data Encryption**: Encrypt data stored in the database using a secure encryption algorithm.

**Scalability**

1. **Load Balancing**: Use load balancing to distribute traffic across multiple servers.
2. **Database Sharding**: Shard the database into smaller, more manageable pieces to improve performance under heavy load.
3. **Caching**: Use caching mechanisms such as Redis to reduce the load on the database.

**Performance Optimization**

1. **Optimize Database Queries**: Optimize database queries to reduce the time it takes to retrieve data.
2. **Use Efficient Data Structures**: Use efficient data structures such as hashing tables to improve lookup times.
3. **Leverage Caching**: Leverage caching mechanisms such as Redis to reduce the load on the database.

**Monitoring and Maintenance**

1. **Monitor API Performance**: Monitor API performance using tools such as New Relic or Datadog.
2. **Track Error Rates**: Track error rates and identify areas for improvement.
3. **Perform Regular Backups**: Perform regular backups of the database to prevent data loss in case of an outage.

By following this design, we can create a scalable, secure, and high-performance URL shortener system that meets the needs of our users.
```

```spl
-- Recipe 17: Tree of Thought
-- Explores multiple reasoning paths in parallel, evaluates each, and selects the best.
-- Each path is explored by a different model, maximising reasoning diversity.
-- Demonstrates branching exploration, comparative evaluation, and path scoring.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 problem="How should a startup prioritize between growth and profitability?"
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama problem="Design a caching strategy for a high-traffic API"
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama problem="Should we rewrite the legacy system or incrementally refactor?"
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama problem="..." model_a=gemma3 model_b=phi4 model_c=qwen2.5

WORKFLOW tree_of_thought
    INPUT:
        @problem TEXT,
        @model_a TEXT DEFAULT 'gemma3',
        @model_b TEXT DEFAULT 'phi4',
        @model_c TEXT DEFAULT 'qwen2.5'
    OUTPUT: @best_solution TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Tree of thought | problem: {@problem} | models: {@model_a}, {@model_b}, {@model_c}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Generate multiple initial approaches — each model reasons independently
    LOGGING f'Generating 3 initial approaches ({@model_a}, {@model_b}, {@model_c}) ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE approach_1(@problem) USING MODEL @model_a INTO @path_a
    GENERATE approach_2(@problem) USING MODEL @model_b INTO @path_b
    GENERATE approach_3(@problem) USING MODEL @model_c INTO @path_c

    -- Step 2: Develop each approach one level deeper (same model per path)
    LOGGING 'Developing all 3 paths ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE develop(@path_a, @problem) USING MODEL @model_a INTO @path_a_developed
    GENERATE develop(@path_b, @problem) USING MODEL @model_b INTO @path_b_developed
    GENERATE develop(@path_c, @problem) USING MODEL @model_c INTO @path_c_developed

    -- Step 3: Score each path on multiple criteria
    LOGGING 'Scoring paths ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE evaluate_path(@path_a_developed, @problem) INTO @score_a
    GENERATE evaluate_path(@path_b_developed, @problem) INTO @score_b
    GENERATE evaluate_path(@path_c_developed, @problem) INTO @score_c

    -- Step 4: Select the best path
    LOGGING f'Scores: A={@score_a} B={@score_b} C={@score_c} — selecting best ...' LEVEL INFO
    GENERATE select_best(
        @path_a_developed, @score_a,
        @path_b_developed, @score_b,
        @path_c_developed, @score_c
    ) INTO @best_path

    -- Step 5: Refine the winning path into a final solution
    LOGGING 'Refining winning path ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE refine_solution(@best_path, @problem) INTO @best_solution

    -- Step 6: Verify the solution holds up
    GENERATE verify(@best_solution, @problem) INTO @verification
    LOGGING f'Verification result: {@verification}' LEVEL INFO

    EVALUATE @verification
        WHEN 'sound' THEN
            COMMIT @best_solution WITH status = 'complete', paths_explored = 3
        ELSE
            -- If verification fails, combine insights from all paths
            LOGGING 'Verification failed — synthesizing all paths' LEVEL WARN
            GENERATE synthesize_all(
                @path_a_developed, @path_b_developed, @path_c_developed, @problem
            ) INTO @best_solution
            COMMIT @best_solution WITH status = 'synthesized', paths_explored = 3
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        -- Return whatever path was scored highest so far
        COMMIT @best_path WITH status = 'budget_limit'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1 LIMIT 3
END
```

```spl
-- Recipe 18: Guardrails Pipeline
-- Input validation → safe generation → output validation → response.
-- Demonstrates safety-first patterns: content filtering, PII detection, and output sanitization.
-- Four gates before a response is committed.
--
-- Usage (from catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=safe_encryption
--
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=pii_ssn
--
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=harmful_malware
--
-- Usage (ad-hoc — no catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       user_input="My SSN is 123-45-6789, help me file taxes"
--
-- Discover available test inputs:
--   spl run ... input_id=list

WORKFLOW guardrails_pipeline
    INPUT:  @input_id   TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @user_input TEXT DEFAULT ''
    OUTPUT: @safe_response TEXT
    SECURITY: CLASSIFICATION: internal
DO
    -- Step 1: Load test input from catalog (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- If input_id is blank, @user_input is used directly in subsequent steps.
    CALL load_test_input(@input_id) INTO @test_context

    -- Gate 1a: Keyword pre-screen (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- Fast check for obvious harmful / off-topic patterns before spending tokens.
    CALL classify_input_keywords(@user_input) INTO @keyword_class

    EVALUATE @keyword_class
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'harmful' THEN
            COMMIT 'I cannot help with that request.' WITH
                status        = 'blocked_harmful',
                gate          = 'keyword_prescreen',
                keyword_match = @keyword_class
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'off_topic' THEN
            COMMIT 'That question is outside my scope. Please ask something relevant.' WITH
                status        = 'blocked_off_topic',
                gate          = 'keyword_prescreen',
                keyword_match = @keyword_class
        ELSE
            -- Continue to LLM classification
    END

    -- Gate 1b: LLM input content classification (nuanced cases keywords miss)
    GENERATE classify_input(@user_input, @test_context) INTO @input_class

    EVALUATE @input_class
        WHEN 'harmful' THEN
            COMMIT 'I cannot help with that request.' WITH
                status = 'blocked_harmful',
                gate   = 'llm_classifier'
        WHEN 'off_topic' THEN
            COMMIT 'That question is outside my scope. Please ask something relevant.' WITH
                status = 'blocked_off_topic',
                gate   = 'llm_classifier'
        ELSE
            -- Input is acceptable, continue
    END

    -- Gate 2: PII detection (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL detect_pii(@user_input) INTO @pii_report

    EVALUATE @pii_report
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'pii_found' THEN
            -- Gate 2b: Redact PII (deterministic — no LLM)
            CALL redact_pii(@user_input) INTO @clean_input
        ELSE
            SET @clean_input = @user_input
    END

    -- Gate 3: Generate response with safety-tuned prompt
    GENERATE safe_response(@clean_input) INTO @raw_response

    -- Gate 4: Output validation — check for PII leakage or harmful content
    GENERATE validate_output(@raw_response, @clean_input) INTO @output_check

    EVALUATE @output_check
        WHEN 'safe' THEN
            SET @safe_response = @raw_response
        WHEN 'contains_pii' THEN
            -- Deterministic PII redaction on the output too
            CALL redact_pii(@raw_response) INTO @safe_response
        WHEN 'hallucination' THEN
            GENERATE safe_response(@clean_input) INTO @safe_response
            RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1 LIMIT 3
        ELSE
            SET @safe_response = 'I was unable to generate a safe response. Please rephrase your question.'
    END

    COMMIT @safe_response WITH
        status       = 'complete',
        input_class  = @input_class,
        pii_detected = @pii_report

EXCEPTION
    WHEN RefusalToAnswer THEN
        COMMIT 'This request cannot be processed.' WITH status = 'refused'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        COMMIT 'Unable to provide a verified response.' WITH status = 'hallucination_blocked'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @safe_response WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     result: FAILED  (2.3s)

```spl
-- Recipe 19: Memory-Augmented Conversation
-- A conversational agent that remembers facts from prior turns using persistent memory.
-- Demonstrates STORAGE-typed workflow inputs, context accumulation, and stateful interactions.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama \
--       'user_input=My name is Alice and I am a data scientist'
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama \
--       'user_input=What is my name?'
--
-- Memory is a first-class INPUT parameter — declared, injected, portable.
-- Override the default path at call time: memory=path/to/other.db
--
-- Memory management:
--   Inspect:  spl memory get chat_user_profile
--   Reset:    spl memory delete chat_user_profile && spl memory delete chat_history

-- Extract any personal facts the user shared. Return the exact string 'no_new_facts'
-- if nothing new was shared, otherwise return a bullet list of facts.
CREATE FUNCTION extract_facts(user_input TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$

You are a fact extractor. Read the user message and extract any personal facts they shared
about themselves (name, job, preferences, location, etc.).

If no personal facts are present, reply with exactly: no_new_facts
Otherwise reply with a concise bullet list, one fact per line, e.g.:
- Name: Alice
- Role: data scientist
- Preference: Python over R

User message: "{user_input}"
$$;

-- Merge new facts into an existing profile. Return the updated profile as a bullet list.
CREATE FUNCTION merge_profile(existing_profile TEXT, new_facts TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$

You are maintaining a user profile as a concise bullet list.
If the existing profile is empty or blank, start a new profile from the new facts only.
Otherwise merge the new facts in, keeping all prior facts unless directly contradicted.
Do NOT invent or assume any facts not explicitly stated.
Return ONLY the bullet list, no preamble.

Existing profile:
{existing_profile}

New facts to merge:
{new_facts}
$$;

-- Reply using full context: known profile + conversation history + current message.
CREATE FUNCTION contextual_reply(user_input TEXT, profile TEXT, history TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$

Known facts about the user: {profile}

Recent conversation:
{history}

Based solely on the known facts above, answer in one sentence: "{user_input}"
If the fact is not listed above, respond: "I don't have that in memory."
$$;

WORKFLOW memory_conversation
    INPUT:
        @user_input TEXT,
        @memory     STORAGE(sqlite, '~/.spl/memory.db')
    OUTPUT: @response TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Memory conversation | input: {@user_input}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Load existing user profile and chat history from the injected storage backend
    @profile      := @memory['chat_user_profile']
    @chat_history := @memory['chat_history']
    LOGGING f'Profile loaded: {@profile}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Extract any new personal facts the user shared
    GENERATE extract_facts(@user_input) INTO @new_facts
    LOGGING f'New facts: {@new_facts}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Update profile only when new facts were actually found (exact string check)
    EVALUATE @new_facts
        WHEN = 'no_new_facts' THEN
            LOGGING 'No new facts — profile unchanged' LEVEL DEBUG
        ELSE
            LOGGING 'New facts detected — merging profile' LEVEL DEBUG
            GENERATE merge_profile(@profile, @new_facts) INTO @profile
            @memory['chat_user_profile'] := @profile
    END

    -- Generate response with full context: profile + history + current input
    GENERATE contextual_reply(@user_input, @profile, @chat_history) INTO @response

    -- Persist updated chat history (keep last 10 turns, deterministic — no LLM cost)
    @chat_history := @chat_history || '\nUser: ' || @user_input || '\nAssistant: ' || @response
    CALL trim_turns(@chat_history, '10') INTO @chat_history
    @memory['chat_history'] := @chat_history

    LOGGING 'Response ready' LEVEL INFO
    COMMIT @response WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT 'I remember you! But I ran out of budget for this response.' WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

```spl
-- Recipe 20: Ensemble Voting
-- Generates multiple independent answers, then uses majority voting to pick the best.
-- Demonstrates ensemble methods, comparative scoring, and consensus-driven output.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 question="What causes inflation?"
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama question="Is Rust faster than C++?"
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama question="What is the best database for time-series data?"

WORKFLOW ensemble_voting
    INPUT: @question TEXT
    OUTPUT: @final_answer TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Ensemble voting | question: {@question}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Generate 5 independent candidate answers with varying temperatures
    LOGGING 'Generating 5 candidate answers ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_1
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_2
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_3
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_4
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_5
    LOGGING '5 candidates ready — scoring ...' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 2: Score each candidate on accuracy, completeness, and clarity
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_1, @question) INTO @score_1
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_2, @question) INTO @score_2
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_3, @question) INTO @score_3
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_4, @question) INTO @score_4
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_5, @question) INTO @score_5
    LOGGING f'Scores: 1={@score_1} 2={@score_2} 3={@score_3} 4={@score_4} 5={@score_5}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 3: Find consensus — identify common themes across candidates
    LOGGING 'Finding consensus ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE find_consensus(
        @candidate_1, @candidate_2, @candidate_3,
        @candidate_4, @candidate_5
    ) INTO @consensus

    -- Step 4: Select the best candidate based on scores and consensus alignment
    LOGGING 'Selecting winner ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE select_winner(
        @candidate_1, @score_1,
        @candidate_2, @score_2,
        @candidate_3, @score_3,
        @candidate_4, @score_4,
        @candidate_5, @score_5,
        @consensus
    ) INTO @best_candidate

    -- Step 5: Polish the winning answer, incorporating consensus insights
    GENERATE polish(@best_candidate, @consensus, @question) INTO @final_answer

    LOGGING 'Final answer ready' LEVEL INFO
    COMMIT @final_answer WITH status = 'complete', candidates = 5

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        -- If budget runs out, use whatever candidates we have
        GENERATE select_winner(
            @candidate_1, @score_1,
            @candidate_2, @score_2,
            @candidate_3, @score_3
        ) INTO @final_answer
        COMMIT @final_answer WITH status = 'partial', candidates = 3
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1 LIMIT 3
END
```

     cmd : spl run ./21_multi_model_pipeline/multi_model.spl --adapter momagrid topic=climate change

```bash
spl run ./21_multi_model_pipeline/multi_model.spl --adapter momagrid topic=climate change
```

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 21: Multi-Model Pipeline
-- Showcases GENERATE ... USING MODEL per-step model selection
-- and semantic WHILE loop with variable context.
--
-- Each step can target a different model — the right model for each task.
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/21_multi_model_pipeline/multi_model.spl --adapter ollama topic="climate change"

CREATE FUNCTION research(topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT 
AS $$

You are a research specialist.
Provide key facts, statistics, and recent developments about: {topic}
Focus on factual accuracy. Be thorough but concise.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION analyze(facts TEXT) RETURNS TEXT 
AS $$

You are a data analyst.
Analyze the following research and identify the 3 most significant insights:

{facts}

For each insight, explain why it matters and rate its significance (1-10).
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION write_summary(analysis TEXT) RETURNS TEXT 
AS $$

You are a professional writer.
Write a clear, engaging 2-paragraph summary based on this analysis:

{analysis}

The first paragraph should present the key findings.
The second paragraph should discuss implications and outlook.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION quality_check(text TEXT) RETURNS TEXT 
AS $$

You are a quality reviewer.
Rate the following text on a scale of 0.0 to 1.0 for:
- Clarity
- Accuracy
- Completeness

Return ONLY a single number (the average score).

Text: {text}
$$;

WORKFLOW multi_model_pipeline
  INPUT:
    @topic TEXT,
    @max_iterations INT DEFAULT 3,
    @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/21_multi_model_pipeline/logs'
  OUTPUT: @final TEXT
DO
  LOGGING f'Multi-model pipeline | topic={@topic}' LEVEL INFO

  -- Step 1: Research with a fast model (good for factual retrieval)
  GENERATE research(@topic) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @facts
  LOGGING 'Research complete' LEVEL DEBUG
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/research.md', @facts) INTO @_

  -- Step 2: Analysis with a reasoning model
  GENERATE analyze(@facts) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @analysis
  LOGGING 'Analysis complete' LEVEL DEBUG
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/analysis.md', @analysis) INTO @_

  -- Step 3: Writing with a creative model
  GENERATE write_summary(@analysis) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @draft
  LOGGING 'Initial draft ready' LEVEL INFO
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/draft_0.md', @draft) INTO @_

  -- Step 4: Quality loop — refine until score > 0.7 (max 3 iterations)
  @iteration := 0
  @quality := 0

  WHILE @iteration < @max_iterations DO
    LOGGING f'Quality check | iteration={@iteration}' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE quality_check(@draft) INTO @quality
    LOGGING f'Quality score: {@quality}' LEVEL DEBUG

    EVALUATE @quality
      WHEN > 0.7 THEN
        LOGGING f'Quality threshold met | score={@quality}' LEVEL INFO
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @draft) INTO @_
        COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'high_quality', score = @quality
      ELSE
        GENERATE write_summary(@analysis) INTO @draft
        @iteration := @iteration + 1
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/draft_{@iteration}.md', @draft) INTO @_
    END
  END

  -- Fallback: commit whatever we have
  LOGGING f'Max iterations reached | final score={@quality}' LEVEL WARN
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @draft) INTO @_
  COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'max_iterations', score = @quality

EXCEPTION
  WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
    COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'partial'
  WHEN ModelOverloaded THEN
    COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'model_overloaded'
END
```
     | 
     | [INFO] Multi-model pipeline | topic=climate change
     | [INFO] Initial draft ready
     | [INFO] Quality threshold met | score=0.8
     | ============================================================
     | Caller:  momagrid-hub
     | Adapter: momagrid
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 4
     | Tokens: 2138 in / 1938 out
     | Latency: 47118ms
     | Timestamp: 2026-03-27 09:11:31
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output
Here’s a two-paragraph summary based on the provided climate change analysis, designed to be clear, engaging, and impactful:

Recent research has delivered a stark and unequivocal picture of our changing climate. 2023 was confirmed as the hottest year on record, registering a staggering 1.48°C above pre-industrial levels – a critical confirmation that climate models are accurate and the impacts are unfolding with alarming speed. This unprecedented warmth isn’t an isolated event; it’s a tangible manifestation of a rapidly escalating trend with profound consequences for the planet. Furthermore, the Arctic is warming at roughly twice the global average rate, a phenomenon known as Arctic Amplification, which is triggering a dangerous cascade of effects, from thawing permafrost releasing potent greenhouse gases to disrupting global weather patterns.

These key insights – the record-breaking warmth, Arctic Amplification, and the rapid depletion of our carbon budget – paint a picture of immediate urgency and challenge. The dwindling carbon budget underscores the critical need for drastic, accelerated emissions reductions across all sectors. The implications extend far beyond simply “warming”; they represent a finite resource and a ticking clock.  Moving forward, continued monitoring and robust action are essential to mitigate the worst effects of climate change and secure a sustainable future.
```

     | 
     | * **Definition:** Climate change refers to long-term shifts in temperatures and weather patterns. These shifts may be natural, but since the mid-19th century, the dominant cause is human activity, particularly the burning of fossil fuels.
     | * **Greenhouse Effect:** The Earth’s atmosphere contains greenhouse gases (GHGs) – carbon dioxide (CO2), methane (CH4), nitrous oxide (N2O), and fluorinated gases. These gases trap heat, warming the planet.
     | * **Human Contribution:**  Around 97% of recent warming is attributed to human influence, according to the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC).
     | * **Key Gases:**
     |     * **CO2:** Primarily from burning fossil fuels (coal, oil, natural gas) and deforestation.  Current atmospheric concentration is around 420 parts per million (ppm) - significantly higher than pre-industrial levels.

     | 
     | * **Global Temperature Rise:** The Earth has warmed by approximately 1.1°C (2.0°F) since the pre-industrial era (1850-1900). The vast majority of this warming has occurred since the 1970s.
     | * **Sea Level Rise:**  Sea levels are rising due to thermal expansion of water (as it warms) and melting of glaciers and ice sheets. The rate of rise has accelerated in recent decades.  Globally, sea levels have risen by about 21-26 centimeters (8-10 inches) since 1880.

```spl
-- Recipe 23: Structured Output
-- Extract typed, structured data from free-form text using CREATE FUNCTION with JSON schema.
-- Demonstrates schema-constrained generation — the LLM must return valid JSON matching the spec.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/23_structured_output/structured_output.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="John Smith, 42, joined Acme Corp in March 2021 earning $95,000/year"
--
--   spl run cookbook/23_structured_output/structured_output.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="Invoice #INV-2045 from TechSupplies Ltd dated 15 Jan 2024 for $3,420.50 due in 30 days"

-- Define the extraction schema
CREATE FUNCTION extract_entity_schema()
RETURNS JSON
AS $$
{
  "type": "object",
  "properties": {
    "people": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "name":       { "type": "string" },
          "age":        { "type": "integer" },
          "role":       { "type": "string" },
          "salary":     { "type": "number" },
          "start_date": { "type": "string", "format": "date" }
        },
        "required": ["name"]
      }
    },
    "organizations": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "name":     { "type": "string" },
          "type":     { "type": "string" },
          "industry": { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["name"]
      }
    },
    "amounts": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "value":    { "type": "number" },
          "currency": { "type": "string", "default": "USD" },
          "label":    { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["value"]
      }
    },
    "dates": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "value": { "type": "string" },
          "label": { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["value"]
      }
    }
  }
}
$$;

PROMPT extract_entities
SELECT
    system_role('You are a precise data extraction engine. Extract all entities from the text and return valid JSON matching the provided schema. Return only the JSON object, no explanation.'),
    context.text AS text,
    extract_entity_schema() AS schema
GENERATE structured_extraction(text, schema)
```

```spl
-- Recipe 24: Few-Shot Prompting
-- Embed gold-standard examples directly in the SELECT context to guide output format and style.
-- Demonstrates in-context learning — no fine-tuning required.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/24_few_shot/few_shot.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="The quarterly results exceeded all analyst forecasts by a significant margin"
--
--   spl run cookbook/24_few_shot/few_shot.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="System outage detected in EU-WEST-2 affecting 15% of users" \
--       domain="ops"

-- Gold-standard examples for tone/format calibration
CREATE FUNCTION few_shot_examples(domain TEXT DEFAULT 'general')
RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
SELECT CASE domain
  WHEN 'finance' THEN
    '### Examples

Input: "Revenue grew 12% YoY despite headwinds in APAC."
Output: {"sentiment": "positive", "magnitude": 0.7, "topics": ["revenue", "growth", "APAC"], "summary": "Revenue up 12% YoY."}

Input: "Write-downs of $2.3B drove a net loss for Q3."
Output: {"sentiment": "negative", "magnitude": 0.9, "topics": ["write-downs", "net loss", "Q3"], "summary": "Q3 net loss from $2.3B write-downs."}'

  WHEN 'ops' THEN
    '### Examples

Input: "Deployment succeeded. All health checks passing. P99 latency 45ms."
Output: {"status": "healthy", "severity": "none", "action_required": false, "summary": "Deploy OK, latency nominal."}

Input: "Pod crash-loop in prod-east. OOMKilled. Restarted 7 times in 10 min."
Output: {"status": "incident", "severity": "high", "action_required": true, "summary": "OOM crash-loop in prod-east, immediate attention needed."}'

  ELSE
    '### Examples

Input: "The new feature launched today and users are loving it!"
Output: {"sentiment": "positive", "magnitude": 0.8, "summary": "Successful feature launch with positive user reception."}

Input: "We encountered several critical bugs that delayed the release by two weeks."
Output: {"sentiment": "negative", "magnitude": 0.7, "summary": "Critical bugs caused two-week release delay."}'
END
$$;

PROMPT few_shot_classifier
SELECT
    system_role('You are a precise text classifier. Follow the examples exactly — same JSON structure, same field names. Return only the JSON object.'),
    few_shot_examples(context.domain) AS examples,
    context.text AS text,
    context.domain AS domain
GENERATE classify(text, examples)
```

```spl
-- Recipe 25: Nested Procedures
-- Demonstrates PROCEDURE calling PROCEDURE — deep composability without external orchestration.
-- Pattern: a high-level workflow delegates subtasks to reusable PROCEDURE units.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/25_nested_procs/nested_procs.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       topic="quantum computing" audience="high school students"
--
--   spl run cookbook/25_nested_procs/nested_procs.spl --adapter ollama \
--       topic="CRISPR gene editing" audience="policy makers" depth="deep"

-- Inner PROCEDURE: generate a single explanation layer
PROCEDURE explain_layer(content TEXT, audience TEXT, style TEXT DEFAULT 'clear and engaging')
RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE explain(content, audience, style) INTO @explanation
    COMMIT @explanation
END

-- Inner PROCEDURE: generate a concrete example for a concept
PROCEDURE make_example(concept TEXT, context TEXT, audience TEXT)
RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE concrete_example(concept, context, audience) INTO @example
    COMMIT @example
END

-- Inner PROCEDURE: check comprehension level and simplify if needed
PROCEDURE calibrate_complexity(text TEXT, audience TEXT, target_grade INT DEFAULT 8)
RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE assess_reading_level(text) INTO @reading_level
    EVALUATE @reading_level
        WHEN > target_grade THEN
            GENERATE simplify(text, audience) INTO @calibrated
            COMMIT @calibrated
        ELSE
            COMMIT text
    END
END

-- Outer WORKFLOW: orchestrates the inner procedures
WORKFLOW layered_explainer
    INPUT:  @topic    TEXT,
            @audience TEXT,
            @depth    TEXT DEFAULT 'standard'
    OUTPUT: @article TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Research the topic
    GENERATE research_overview(@topic) INTO @overview

    -- Step 2: Delegate explanation to inner procedure
    CALL explain_layer(
        content  = @overview,
        audience = @audience,
        style    = 'clear, engaging, avoid jargon'
    ) INTO @base_explanation

    -- Step 3: Generate a concrete example via another inner procedure
    CALL make_example(
        concept  = @topic,
        context  = @base_explanation,
        audience = @audience
    ) INTO @example

    -- Step 4: Calibrate complexity (nested call inside a conditional)
    CALL calibrate_complexity(
        text         = @base_explanation,
        audience     = @audience,
        target_grade = 8
    ) INTO @calibrated_explanation

    -- Step 5: Assemble the final article
    GENERATE assemble_article(
        @topic,
        @calibrated_explanation,
        @example,
        @depth
    ) INTO @article

    COMMIT @article WITH status = 'complete', audience = @audience
END
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Caller:  momagrid-hub
     | Adapter: momagrid
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 5
     | Tokens: 3284 in / 3199 out
     | Latency: 63546ms
     | Timestamp: 2026-03-27 09:13:03

```output
Here is a formatted article based on the input:

**What is Quantum Computing?**

Imagine a computer that's like the most powerful supercomputer in the world, but it can solve problems way faster and more accurately than any regular computer. This is what quantum computing is all about!

Quantum computers use special tiny particles called qubits to process information. Unlike regular bits, which can only be either 0 or 1, qubits can be both at the same time! This means that quantum computers can try many possibilities all at once, making them incredibly fast.

**How Does it Work?**

Think of a big library with millions of books. A classical computer would have to look through each book one by one to find the answer it's looking for. A quantum computer, on the other hand, can look at all the books at the same time and find the right answer instantly!

**What Can Quantum Computing Do?**

Quantum computers are super good at solving certain types of problems, like:

1. **Cryptography**: They can break some secret codes that regular computers can't.
2. **Optimization**: They can help find the best way to do things, like optimize traffic flow or supply chains.
3. **Simulation**: They can simulate real-world systems, like molecular interactions and chemical reactions.

**Why is it Important?**

Quantum computing has the potential to solve some of humanity's biggest challenges, like:

1. **Curing diseases**: By simulating how molecules interact, quantum computers might help us find new medicines.
2. **Optimizing energy usage**: They could help us make our homes and cities more efficient and sustainable.
3. **Breaking encryption codes**: Quantum computers could help keep our online information safe from hackers.

**What are the Challenges?**

While quantum computing is exciting, there are some big challenges to overcome:

1. **Stability**: Qubits can get damaged easily, which makes it hard for them to work correctly.
2. **Scalability**: Right now, most quantum computers are really small and need to be scaled up to solve real-world problems.
3. **Error correction**: We need to find ways to fix mistakes that happen during calculations.

**What's Next?**

Researchers are working hard to improve quantum computing technology. Some of the exciting things on the horizon include:

1. **Quantum supremacy**: Demonstrating that a quantum computer can solve a problem that regular computers can't.
2. **Hybrid systems**: Combining classical and quantum computing for even more powerful results.
3. **Post-quantum cryptography**: Developing new encryption techniques that will be secure even against future quantum threats.

**Conclusion**

Quantum computing is like having the most powerful supercomputer in the world, but with a twist! It's still a young technology, but it has the potential to solve some of humanity's biggest challenges. As researchers and developers continue to work on improving quantum computing, we can expect big breakthroughs in the years to come.

**Tips for Explaining Quantum Computing to High School Students:**

1. Use simple analogies like the coin flip example.
2. Focus on the "wow" factor of quantum computers' speed and capabilities.
3. Emphasize the potential impact on real-world problems, like curing diseases or optimizing energy usage.
4. Avoid getting too technical or overwhelming with complex concepts.
5. Encourage curiosity and ask questions to spark their interest!

**Concrete Example: Explaining Quantum Computing to High School Students**

Imagine you're trying to find a specific book in a huge library with millions of books. A regular computer would have to look through each book one by one, but a quantum computer can look at all the books at the same time!

Quantum computers use special tiny particles called qubits to process information. Unlike regular bits, which can only be either 0 or 1, qubits can be both at the same time! This means that quantum computers can try many possibilities all at once, making them incredibly fast.

**How Does it Work?**

Think of a coin flip. A classical computer would have to try each possible outcome (heads or tails) one by one. A quantum computer, on the other hand, can try both outcomes at the same time!

**What Can Quantum Computing Do?**

Quantum computers are super good at solving certain types of problems, like:

1. **Cryptography**: They can break some secret codes that regular computers can't.
2. **Optimization**: They can help find the best way to do things, like optimize traffic flow or supply chains.
3. **Simulation**: They can simulate real-world systems, like molecular interactions and chemical reactions.

**Why is it Important?**

Quantum computing has the potential to solve some of humanity's biggest challenges, like:

1. **Curing diseases**: By simulating how molecules interact, quantum computers might help us find new medicines.
2. **Optimizing energy usage**: They could
```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @topic = quantum computing
     |   @audience = high school students
     |   @depth = standard
     |   @overview = **Research Overview: Quantum Computing**
     | 
     | Quantum computing is a revolutionary technology that utilizes the principles of quantum mechanics to perform calculations and operations on data. It has the potential to solve complex problems exponentially faster than classical computers, making it an attractive field for research and development.
     | 
     | **Key Concepts:**
     | 
     | 1. **Qubits**: Quantum bits are the fundamental units of quantum information, which can exist in multiple states simultaneously (superposition) and be entangled with other qubits.
     | 2. **Quantum gates**: These are the quantum equivalent of logic gates in classical computing, performing operations on qubits such as rotation, entanglement, and measurement.
     | 3. **Quantum algorithms**: These are programs designed to take advantage of quantum parallelism to solve specific problems, such as Shor's algorithm for factorization and Grover's algorithm for search.
     | 
     | **Types of Quantum Computing:**
     | 
     | 1. **Gate-based quantum computing**: This is the most common type, where qubits are manipulated using a series of quantum gates.
     | 2. **Analog quantum computing**: This type uses continuous-variable qubits to perform calculations on analog signals.
     | 3. **Topological quantum computing**: This approach uses exotic materials and topological phases to store quantum information.
     | 
     | **Applications:**
     | 
     | 1. **Cryptography**: Quantum computers can break certain classical encryption algorithms, but they also offer new opportunities for quantum-resistant cryptography.
     | 2. **Optimization**: Quantum computers can solve complex optimization problems in fields like logistics, finance, and energy management.
     | 3. **Simulation**: Quantum computers can simulate complex systems, such as molecular interactions and chemical reactions, leading to breakthroughs in materials science and pharmaceutical research.
     | 
     | **Challenges:**
     | 
     | 1. **Error correction**: Quantum computers are prone to errors due to the fragile nature of qubits.
     | 2. **Scalability**: Currently, most quantum computers are small-scale and need to be scaled up to solve real-world problems.
     | 3. **Quantum noise**: Random fluctuations in the quantum system can cause errors and instability.
     | 
     | **Current State:**
     | 
     | 1. **IBM Quantum Experience**: A cloud-based platform offering access to a 53-qubit quantum computer for researchers and developers.
     | 2. **Google Quantum AI Lab**: A research-focused platform providing access to a 72-qubit quantum computer.
     | 3. **Quantum computing startups**: Companies like Rigetti Computing, IonQ, and Xanadu are actively developing their own quantum computing technologies.
     | 
     | **Future Directions:**
     | 
     | 1. **Quantum supremacy**: Demonstrating the ability of a quantum computer to solve a problem that is beyond the capabilities of classical computers.
     | 2. **Quantum-classical hybrids**: Developing systems that combine the strengths of both quantum and classical computing.
     | 3. **Post-quantum cryptography**: Developing new cryptographic techniques that can resist attacks from potential quantum computers.
     | 
     | Overall, quantum computing has the potential to revolutionize various fields by solving complex problems efficiently. However, significant technical challenges need to be addressed before these systems can be widely adopted.
     |   @base_explanation = **What is Quantum Computing?**
     | 
     | Imagine a computer that's like the most powerful supercomputer in the world, but it can solve problems way faster and more accurately than any regular computer. This is what quantum computing is all about!
     | 
     | Quantum computers use special tiny particles called qubits to process information. Unlike regular bits, which can only be either 0 or 1, qubits can be both at the same time! This means that quantum computers can try many possibilities all at once, making them incredibly fast.
     | 
     | **How Does it Work?**
     | 
     | Think of a big library with millions of books. A classical computer would have to look through each book one by one to find the answer it's looking for. A quantum computer, on the other hand, can look at all the books at the same time and find the right answer instantly!
     | 
     | **What Can Quantum Computing Do?**
     | 
     | Quantum computers are super good at solving certain types of problems, like:
     | 
     | 1. **Cryptography**: They can break some secret codes that regular computers can't.
     | 2. **Optimization**: They can help find the best way to do things, like optimize traffic flow or supply chains.
     | 3. **Simulation**: They can simulate real-world systems, like molecular interactions and chemical reactions.
     | 
     | **Why is it Important?**
     | 
     | Quantum computing has the potential to solve some of humanity's biggest challenges, like:
     | 
     | 1. **Curing diseases**: By simulating how molecules interact, quantum computers might help us find new medicines.
     | 2. **Optimizing energy usage**: They could help us make our homes and cities more efficient and sustainable.
     | 3. **Breaking encryption codes**: Quantum computers could help keep our online information safe from hackers.
     | 
     | **What are the Challenges?**
     | 
     | While quantum computing is exciting, there are some big challenges to overcome:
     | 
     | 1. **Stability**: Qubits can get damaged easily, which makes it hard for them to work correctly.
     | 2. **Scalability**: Right now, most quantum computers are really small and need to be scaled up to solve real-world problems.
     | 3. **Error correction**: We need to find ways to fix mistakes that happen during calculations.
     | 
     | **What's Next?**
     | 
     | Researchers are working hard to improve quantum computing technology. Some of the exciting things on the horizon include:
     | 
     | 1. **Quantum supremacy**: Demonstrating that a quantum computer can solve a problem that regular computers can't.
     | 2. **Hybrid systems**: Combining classical and quantum computing for even more powerful results.
     | 3. **Post-quantum cryptography**: Developing new encryption techniques that will be secure even against future quantum threats.
     | 
     | **Conclusion**
     | 
     | Quantum computing is like having the most powerful supercomputer in the world, but with a twist! It's still a young technology, but it has the potential to solve some of humanity's biggest challenges. As researchers and developers continue to work on improving quantum computing, we can expect big breakthroughs in the years to come.

```spl

-- Recipe 26: Prompt A/B Test
-- Compare two prompt variants using EVALUATE scoring. Pick the winner automatically.
-- Demonstrates data-driven prompt engineering built into the workflow itself.
-- Supports pre-built experiments from experiments.json or ad-hoc task + prompt_a + prompt_b.
--
-- Usage (from catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/26_ab_test/tools.py \
--       experiment_id=neural_networks
--
--   spl run cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/26_ab_test/tools.py \
--       experiment_id=standing_desk winner_threshold=2.0
--
-- Usage (ad-hoc — no catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/26_ab_test/tools.py \
--       task="Explain neural networks" \
--       prompt_a="Explain like I'm 5" \
--       prompt_b="Give a technical explanation with analogies"
--
-- Discover available experiments:
--   spl run ... experiment_id=list

-- Scoring rubric as a reusable function
CREATE FUNCTION scoring_rubric()
RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
Rate the response on a scale of 0–10 for each criterion:
1. Clarity (0-10): Is it easy to understand?
2. Completeness (0-10): Does it fully address the task?
3. Relevance (0-10): Is every sentence on-topic?
4. Engagement (0-10): Is it interesting to read?

Return valid JSON only — no prose outside the JSON:
{"clarity": N, "completeness": N, "relevance": N, "engagement": N, "total": N, "rationale": "one concise sentence"}
$$;

WORKFLOW ab_test
    INPUT:  @experiment_id    TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @task             TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @prompt_a         TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @prompt_b         TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @winner_threshold FLOAT DEFAULT 1.5
    OUTPUT: @winner_output TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Load experiment from catalog (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- Sets @experiment_context with task, prompt_a, prompt_b, and notes.
    -- If experiment_id is blank, the context will be empty and @task/@prompt_a/@prompt_b are used directly.
    CALL load_experiment(@experiment_id) INTO @experiment_context

    -- Step 2: Generate Variant A response
    GENERATE run_variant_a(
        @task,
        @prompt_a,
        @experiment_context
    ) INTO @response_a

    -- Step 3: Generate Variant B response
    GENERATE run_variant_b(
        @task,
        @prompt_b,
        @experiment_context
    ) INTO @response_b

    -- Step 4: Score both variants against the rubric (LLM)
    GENERATE evaluate_response(@response_a, @task, scoring_rubric()) INTO @score_a_json
    GENERATE evaluate_response(@response_b, @task, scoring_rubric()) INTO @score_b_json

    -- Step 5: Extract numeric totals (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL extract_score_total(@score_a_json) INTO @score_a
    CALL extract_score_total(@score_b_json) INTO @score_b

    -- Step 6: Pick winner or declare tie
    EVALUATE (@score_a - @score_b)
        WHEN > @winner_threshold THEN
            SET @winner_output = @response_a
            COMMIT @winner_output WITH
                winner    = 'A',
                score_a   = @score_a,
                score_b   = @score_b,
                margin    = (@score_a - @score_b)
        WHEN < (0 - @winner_threshold) THEN
            SET @winner_output = @response_b
            COMMIT @winner_output WITH
                winner    = 'B',
                score_a   = @score_a,
                score_b   = @score_b,
                margin    = (@score_b - @score_a)
        ELSE
            -- Scores too close — format both with explanation (deterministic — no LLM)
            CALL format_tie_result(@response_a, @response_b, @score_a_json, @score_b_json) INTO @winner_output
            COMMIT @winner_output WITH
                winner  = 'tie',
                score_a = @score_a,
                score_b = @score_b
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT 'A/B test failed during generation.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```

```output
  to provide an explanation!

  will choose experiment "neural_networks" to test the classic readability
  vs depth trade-off in prompt A.

**Neural Networks: A Simplified Explanation**

Imagine you're trying to teach a child about how neural networks work. You
  could start with a complex diagram showing multiple layers and connections
  between them. However, this might overwhelm the child and make it
  difficult for them to understand.

A better approach would be to break down the concept into smaller, more
  manageable chunks. Start by explaining what a neuron is, how it processes
  information, and then gradually build up to more complex topics like
  layered networks and training.

Think of it like building a tower with blocks. Each block represents a
  simple concept, and as you add more blocks, the tower grows taller and
  more complex. Just as a strong foundation is essential for a stable tower,
  a solid understanding of individual components is crucial for a robust
  neural network.

**Key Takeaways**

1. **Simplify Complex Concepts**: Break down complex topics into smaller,
  more digestible pieces.
2. **Focus on Key Components**: Identify the most important elements and
  explain them in detail.
3. **Use Analogies and Metaphors**: Use relatable concepts to help readers
  visualize and understand abstract ideas.

By following this approach, you can create an engaging and informative
  explanation that helps readers develop a deeper understanding of neural
  networks.
```

```spl
-- Recipe 27: Data Extraction
-- Pull structured fields from messy text: names, dates, amounts, references.
-- Demonstrates schema-constrained extraction on real-world noisy input.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/27_data_extraction/data_extraction.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="Please process payment of USD 4,250.00 to Riverside Consulting (ref: PO-8821) by end of March."
--
--   spl run cookbook/27_data_extraction/data_extraction.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="$(cat invoice.txt)" format="invoice"

CREATE FUNCTION extraction_schema(format TEXT DEFAULT 'general')
RETURNS JSON
AS $$
SELECT CASE format
  WHEN 'invoice' THEN '{
    "type": "object",
    "properties": {
      "invoice_number": {"type": "string"},
      "vendor":         {"type": "string"},
      "amount":         {"type": "number"},
      "currency":       {"type": "string", "default": "USD"},
      "issue_date":     {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "due_date":       {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "line_items":     {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "object", "properties": {"description": {"type": "string"}, "amount": {"type": "number"}}}},
      "payment_terms":  {"type": "string"},
      "po_reference":   {"type": "string"}
    }
  }'
  WHEN 'contract' THEN '{
    "type": "object",
    "properties": {
      "parties":        {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "effective_date": {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "expiry_date":    {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "value":          {"type": "number"},
      "currency":       {"type": "string"},
      "jurisdiction":   {"type": "string"},
      "key_obligations":{"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}}
    }
  }'
  ELSE '{
    "type": "object",
    "properties": {
      "names":        {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "organizations":{"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "dates":        {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "object", "properties": {"value": {"type": "string"}, "label": {"type": "string"}}}},
      "amounts":      {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "object", "properties": {"value": {"type": "number"}, "currency": {"type": "string"}, "label": {"type": "string"}}}},
      "references":   {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "locations":    {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}}
    }
  }'
END
$$;

PROMPT extract_fields
SELECT
    system_role('You are a data extraction specialist. Extract all relevant fields from the text and return valid JSON matching the schema exactly. If a field is not present in the text, omit it. Return only the JSON object.'),
    context.text AS text,
    extraction_schema(context.format) AS schema,
    context.format AS format
GENERATE extract(text, schema)
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Caller:  momagrid-hub
     | Adapter: momagrid
     | Model: llama3.2
     | Tokens: 118 in / 35 out
     | Latency: 4164ms
     | Cost: $0.000000
     | Timestamp: 2026-03-27 09:13:22
     | ------------------------------------------------------------

```output
{ "paymentAmount": "4250", "receiver": "Riverside Consulting", "reference": "PO-8821", "currency": "USD" }
```

```spl
-- Recipe 28: Customer Support Triage
-- Classify → route → draft response in a single workflow.
-- Demonstrates multi-step pipeline with branching, deterministic tool calls for
-- order lookup, and LLM generation grounded in real order data.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/28_support_triage/support_triage.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/28_support_triage/tools.py \
--       ticket="My account has been charged twice for the same order #ORD-12345"
--
--   spl run cookbook/28_support_triage/support_triage.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/28_support_triage/tools.py \
--       ticket="Package for order ORD-67890 hasn't arrived, it's been 10 days" \
--       product="CloudDash" tone="empathetic"
--
--   spl run cookbook/28_support_triage/support_triage.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/28_support_triage/tools.py \
--       ticket="I can't log in, password reset email never arrives" \
--       tone="friendly"

-- ── Helper functions (evaluated locally, zero LLM cost) ────────────────────

CREATE FUNCTION support_categories()
RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
Ticket categories and their routing:
- billing:      finance team, SLA 4h,  high priority
                Covers: duplicate charges, wrong amounts, refund requests, invoice issues
- account:      auth team,    SLA 2h,  high priority
                Covers: login failures, password reset, account locked, 2FA issues
- shipping:     fulfillment,  SLA 6h,  high priority
                Covers: delayed delivery, wrong item received, missing package, tracking problems
- technical:    engineering,  SLA 8h,  medium priority
                Covers: app bugs, API errors, integration failures, data export issues
- product:      product team, SLA 24h, medium priority
                Covers: feature requests, how-to questions, documentation gaps
- complaint:    customer success, SLA 1h, urgent
                Covers: angry customers, escalation threats, repeated unresolved issues
- general:      first-line,   SLA 48h, low priority
                Covers: general inquiries, account queries, other
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION response_tone_guide(tone TEXT DEFAULT 'professional')
RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
SELECT CASE tone
  WHEN 'empathetic' THEN 'Warm and understanding. Acknowledge frustration. Use "I understand" and "I am sorry". Avoid jargon. Refer to the customer by first name.'
  WHEN 'formal'     THEN 'Professional and precise. Use formal language. Reference ticket numbers and order IDs explicitly. State next steps clearly.'
  WHEN 'friendly'   THEN 'Conversational and positive. Use the customer first name. End with an enthusiastic offer to help further.'
  ELSE                   'Professional but approachable. Concise. Action-oriented. Reference specific order details when available. No excessive apologies.'
END
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION order_context_prompt(order_context TEXT)
RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
SELECT CASE
  WHEN order_context = 'No order number found in this ticket.'
    THEN 'No order number was referenced — respond based on the ticket text alone.'
  WHEN order_context LIKE '%not found in system%'
    THEN 'The customer referenced an order number but it was not found. Ask them to confirm the order ID.'
  ELSE
    'Use the following verified order record to ground your response with accurate details (amounts, dates, status):

' || order_context
END
$$;

-- ── Main workflow ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

WORKFLOW support_triage
    INPUT:  @ticket  TEXT,
            @product TEXT DEFAULT 'CloudDash',
            @tone    TEXT DEFAULT 'professional'
    OUTPUT: @drafted_response TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Support triage | product={@product} tone={@tone}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Extract any order numbers mentioned in the ticket (deterministic tool — no LLM)
    CALL extract_order_numbers(@ticket) INTO @order_numbers

    -- Step 2: Fetch the matching order record(s) from orders.json (deterministic tool — no LLM)
    CALL lookup_order(@order_numbers) INTO @order_context
    LOGGING f'Order context loaded | order_numbers={@order_numbers}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 3: Classify the ticket using categories + order data as grounding context
    GENERATE classify_ticket(@ticket, support_categories(), order_context_prompt(@order_context)) INTO @classification_json
    LOGGING f'Classification: {@classification_json}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 4: Extract key structured details from the ticket
    GENERATE extract_ticket_details(@ticket, @order_context) INTO @details_json

    -- Step 5: Score urgency 0-10 based on ticket text and classification
    GENERATE detect_urgency(@ticket, @classification_json) INTO @urgency_score
    LOGGING f'Urgency score: {@urgency_score}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 6: Route — escalate immediately if urgency > 8, otherwise draft response
    EVALUATE @urgency_score
        WHEN > 8 THEN
            LOGGING f'High urgency — escalating | score={@urgency_score}' LEVEL WARN
            GENERATE escalation_alert(@ticket, @classification_json, @order_context) INTO @alert
            COMMIT @alert WITH status = 'escalated', priority = 'critical'
        ELSE
            -- Step 7: Draft a grounded, tone-appropriate response
            GENERATE draft_response(
                @ticket,
                @classification_json,
                @details_json,
                @order_context,
                @product,
                response_tone_guide(@tone)
            ) INTO @drafted_response

            -- Step 8: Quality-check the draft (0-10); revise once if score < 6
            GENERATE check_response_quality(@drafted_response, @ticket, @order_context) INTO @quality_score
            LOGGING f'Draft quality score: {@quality_score}' LEVEL DEBUG

            EVALUATE @quality_score
                WHEN < 6 THEN
                    LOGGING f'Quality below threshold — revising | score={@quality_score}' LEVEL WARN
                    GENERATE improve_response(@drafted_response, @ticket, @order_context) INTO @drafted_response
                    COMMIT @drafted_response WITH status = 'drafted_revised'
                ELSE
                    COMMIT @drafted_response WITH status = 'drafted', quality = @quality_score
            END
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        GENERATE fallback_response(@ticket, @product) INTO @drafted_response
        COMMIT @drafted_response WITH status = 'fallback'
END
```

```output
def draft_response(input_string):
    # Check if the input string contains an order ID
    if "order #12345" in input_string.lower():
        return f"My account has been charged twice for order #12345. Can you please help me resolve this issue?"
    
    # If no order ID is found, check if the input string mentions CloudDash
    elif "CloudDash" in input_string.lower():
        return "Hello! I'm experiencing some issues with my CloudDash subscription. Could you please assist me?"
    
    # If neither condition is met, use a default response
    else:
        return "Sorry to hear that you're experiencing an issue. Can you please provide more details so we can help you better?"
```

```python
import json

def extract_ticket_details(input_string):
    # Convert input to JSON format if it's a string
    if isinstance(input_string, str):
        data = json.loads(input_string)
    else:
        data = input_string

    orders_found = data.get("orders_found", [])

    for order in orders_found:
        for charge in order["charges"]:
            # Extract relevant details from the charge object
            ticket_details = {
                "order_id": order["order_id"],
                "customer_id": order["customer_id"],
                "customer_name": order["customer_name"],
                "customer_email": order["customer_email"],
                "product_line": order["product_line"],
                "items": [item for item in order["items"]],
                "subtotal": order["subtotal"],
                "tax": order["tax"],
                "total": order["total"],
                "currency": order["currency"],
                "status": order["status"],
                "payment_status": order["payment_status"],
                "charges": charge,
                "shipping_address": order["shipping_address"],
                "tracking_number": order["tracking_number"],
                "created_at": order["created_at"],
                "shipped_at": order.get("shipped_at"),
                "delivered_at": order["delivered_at"],
                "notes": order["notes"]
            }

            # Return the ticket details as a dictionary
            yield ticket_details

# Example usage:
```

     |   @quality_score = The function `draft_response` can be improved to handle different types of input more robustly and efficiently. Here's a refactored version with additional error checking and handling:
     | 
```python
import json

def draft_response(input_string):
    """
    Drafts a response based on the content of the input string.
    
    Args:
        input_string (str): The input string containing information about an order.

    Returns:
        str: A drafted response related to the order.
    """

    # Check if the input is None or not a string
    if not isinstance(input_string, str):
        return "Error: Invalid input. Please enter a valid JSON string."

    try:
        # Try to parse the input as JSON
        data = json.loads(input_string)
        
        # Extract the order ID from the 'orders_found' list
        orders = data.get('orders_found', [])
        if not orders:
            return "Error: No orders found in the input."

        order_id = None
        for o in orders:
            if o.get('order_id'):
                order_id = o['order_id']
                break

        # Check if an order ID is found
        if not order_id:
            return "Error: Order ID not found in the input."

        # If the input string mentions CloudDash, use a default response
        if "CloudDash" in input_string.lower():
            return "Hello! I'm experiencing some issues with my CloudDash subscription. Could you please assist me?"

        # Check for duplicate charges
        elif "duplicate_charge" in o.get('payment_status', '').lower() and order_id:
            return f"My account has been charged twice for order #{order_id}. Can you please help me resolve this issue?"

        # If no special conditions are met, use a default response
        else:
            return "Sorry to hear that you're experiencing an issue. Can you please provide more details so we can help you better?"
    
    except json.JSONDecodeError as e:
        # Handle JSON decoding errors
        return f"Error: Invalid JSON input. {e}"
```

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 29: Meeting Notes → Action Items
-- Transcript in, structured TODO list + owners out.
-- Demonstrates multi-step extraction + structured output from long-form text,
-- with deterministic tool calls for file loading, speaker extraction,
-- date normalisation, and ownership validation.
--
-- Usage (load from file):
--   spl run cookbook/29_meeting_actions/meeting_actions.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/29_meeting_actions/tools.py \
--       filename=sprint_planning.txt \
--       output_format=markdown
--
-- Usage (inline transcript):
--   spl run cookbook/29_meeting_actions/meeting_actions.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/29_meeting_actions/tools.py \
--       transcript="Alice: fix the login bug by Friday. Bob: I will handle it." \
--       output_format=json
--
-- Sample transcripts in transcripts/:
--   sprint_planning.txt   Full sprint planning with assignments and decisions
--   design_review.txt     API architecture review with open questions
--   standup.txt           Short daily standup with blockers

-- ── Schema (zero LLM cost — injected as grounding context) ─────────────────

CREATE FUNCTION action_item_schema()
RETURNS JSON
AS $$
{
  "type": "object",
  "properties": {
    "meeting_title":   {"type": "string"},
    "meeting_date":    {"type": "string"},
    "attendees":       {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
    "meeting_summary": {"type": "string"},
    "decisions": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {"type": "string"}
    },
    "action_items": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "task":     {"type": "string"},
          "owner":    {"type": "string", "description": "Person responsible. Use exact speaker name or empty string if unassigned."},
          "due_date": {"type": "string", "description": "Due date or relative phrase e.g. Friday, next Wednesday, end of sprint"},
          "priority": {"type": "string", "enum": ["high", "medium", "low"]},
          "context":  {"type": "string", "description": "Why this task matters or what triggered it"}
        },
        "required": ["task", "priority"]
      }
    },
    "open_questions": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {"type": "string"}
    },
    "next_meeting": {"type": "string"}
  },
  "required": ["meeting_summary", "action_items"]
}
$$;

-- ── Main workflow ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

WORKFLOW meeting_to_actions
    INPUT:  @filename      TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @transcript    TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @output_format TEXT DEFAULT 'json'
    OUTPUT: @output TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Meeting to actions | format={@output_format} filename={@filename}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Load transcript from file if filename provided (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL load_transcript(@filename) INTO @file_content

    -- Step 2: Extract speaker names for grounding the LLM (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL extract_speakers(@file_content) INTO @speakers_from_file
    CALL extract_speakers(@transcript)   INTO @speakers_from_inline
    LOGGING f'Speakers from file: {@speakers_from_file}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 3: Normalise transcript — merge sources, clean formatting, identify speakers
    GENERATE normalize_transcript(
        @file_content,
        @transcript,
        @speakers_from_file,
        @speakers_from_inline
    ) INTO @clean_transcript
    LOGGING 'Transcript normalised' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 4: Extract structured action items grounded by schema and known speakers
    GENERATE extract_actions(
        @clean_transcript,
        action_item_schema(),
        @speakers_from_file,
        @speakers_from_inline
    ) INTO @structured_json
    LOGGING 'Action items extracted' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 5: Normalise relative due dates to ISO-8601 (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL normalize_dates(@structured_json) INTO @structured_json

    -- Step 6: Validate ownership — flag unowned high-priority items (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL validate_ownership(@structured_json) INTO @validation_notes

    -- Step 7: Format and commit output
    EVALUATE @output_format
        WHEN = 'markdown' THEN
            GENERATE format_as_markdown(@structured_json, @validation_notes) INTO @output
            COMMIT @output WITH status = 'complete', format = 'markdown'
        WHEN = 'email' THEN
            GENERATE format_as_email(@structured_json, @validation_notes) INTO @output
            COMMIT @output WITH status = 'complete', format = 'email'
        ELSE
            SET @output = @structured_json
            COMMIT @output WITH status = 'complete', format = 'json'
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- Long transcript: summarise first, then extract from the summary
        GENERATE summarize_transcript(@clean_transcript) INTO @summary
        GENERATE extract_actions(@summary, action_item_schema(), @speakers_from_file, @speakers_from_inline) INTO @structured_json
        CALL normalize_dates(@structured_json) INTO @structured_json
        CALL validate_ownership(@structured_json) INTO @validation_notes
        SET @output = @structured_json
        COMMIT @output WITH status = 'complete_chunked'
END
```

Here is the formatted code using Markdown:

**Python Solution to Extract Actions from Input Transcript**
===========================================================

### Overview
This Python function takes a transcript as input and extracts actions mentioned by speakers. The actions are identified through regular expressions that match verbs in the message.

### Code

```python
import re

def extract_actions(transcript):
    """
    Extracts actions from the input transcript.

    Args:
        transcript (str): The input transcript.

    Returns:
        list: A list of extracted actions.
    """

    # Initialize an empty list to store extracted actions
    actions = []

    # Iterate over each line in the transcript
    for line in transcript.split('\n'):
        # Check if the line is not empty
        if line:
            match = re.match(r'([A-Za-z\s]+): (.*)', line)
            
            # If a speaker and message are found, extract action from the message
            if match:
                speaker, message = match.groups()
                
                # Convert message to lowercase for easier matching
                message = message.lower()
                
                # Regular expression pattern to match verbs (potential actions)
                verb_pattern = r'\b(\w+)\b'
                verbs = re.findall(verb_pattern, message)
                
                # Add the extracted action to the list
                actions.extend([action for action in verbs if action not in ['need', 'also']])

    return actions

# Test the function
transcript1 = """Alice: we need to fix the login bug before Friday.
Bob: I'll handle it."""
print(extract_actions(transcript1))

transcript2 = "Unknown"
print(extract_actions(transcript2))

transcript3 = """Alice"""
print(extract_actions(transcript3))
```
     | 
     | ### Explanation
     | The function `extract_actions` takes a transcript as input and iterates over each line. It uses regular expressions to match the speaker and message, converts the message to lowercase for easier matching, and identifies potential actions (verbs). The extracted actions are then added to the list of extracted actions.
     | 
     | Note that this code only extracts verbs that are not negating words (`'need', 'also'`), ensuring that only actual actions are included in the output.
     | ```

     |                 # Otherwise, add the speaker to the dictionary with their message as a list

```python
import re

def extract_actions(transcript):
    # Initialize an empty list to store extracted actions
    actions = []

    # Iterate over each line in the transcript
    for line in transcript.split('\n'):
        # Check if the line is not empty
        if line:
            match = re.match(r'([A-Za-z\s]+): (.*)', line)
            
            # If a speaker and message are found, extract action from the message
            if match:
                speaker, message = match.groups()
                
                # Convert message to lowercase for easier matching
                message = message.lower()
                
                # Regular expression pattern to match verbs (potential actions)
                verb_pattern = r'\b(\w+)\b'
                verbs = re.findall(verb_pattern, message)
                
                # Add the extracted action to the list
                actions.extend([action for action in verbs if action not in ['need', 'also']])

    return actions

# Test the function
transcript1 = """Alice: we need to fix the login bug before Friday.
Bob: I'll handle it."""
print(extract_actions(transcript1))

transcript2 = "Unknown"
print(extract_actions(transcript2))

transcript3 = """Alice"""
print(extract_actions(transcript3))
```
     | 
     | In this code, we first use a regular expression to match the speaker and message from each line in the transcript. We then convert the message to lowercase for easier matching with potential actions.
     | 
     | We define a regular expression pattern `verb_pattern` that matches verbs (potential actions) in the message. The `\b` characters ensure that only whole words are matched, preventing partial words from being extracted as actions.
     | 
     | Finally, we iterate over each verb found in the message and add it to the list of extracted actions if it's not a negating word (`'need', 'also'`). This ensures that only actual actions are included in the output.
